Distinguishing Sijill and Kitab Hukmi in Hanafi Qada
Defining two types of judge's letters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define sijill and kitab hukmi, explain the temporal and authority differences between them, and apply the correct terminology to judicial documents.

Core Idea
A sijill records a judgment after it is made; a kitab hukmi transmits testimony before judgment.

In Hanafi judicial procedure, a judge may write two types of letters: the sijill, which is a record of a judgment already pronounced, and the kitab hukmi, which is a transmission of witness testimony to another judge for him to adjudicate. Understanding the distinction is crucial for determining the authority of the recipient judge and the stage at which the case stands.

Guided Path
1
Define the two terms
State the definitions of sijill and kitab hukmi as given in the passage: the sijill is a document containing a judge's judgment after it is pronounced; the kitab hukmi is a letter transmitting testimony before judgment.
2
Identify the temporal relationship
Note that the sijill exists only after the judgment, while the kitab hukmi exists before the judgment. The passage explicitly states: 'السجل لا يكون إلا بعد الحكم، والكتاب الحكمي لا يكون إلا قبل الحكم'.
3
Explain the purpose
A sijill is written to preserve the case (lest the incident be forgotten) and to enforce the judgment when the defendant is absent or denies. A kitab hukmi is written to transmit testimony so that another judge may adjudicate in a different location.
4
Analyze the authority difference
The recipient judge must execute a judgment contained in a sijill; he has no authority to reject or overturn it. For a kitab hukmi, the recipient judge may oppose the writer's opinion and even overturn the judgment.
This difference arises because the sijill already constitutes a binding judgment, while the kitab hukmi is only evidence.
5
Apply to examples
Given a scenario, classify the document as sijill or kitab hukmi and state the corresponding ruling for the recipient judge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب كتاب القاضي وكتب بحكمه وهو المدعو سجلاً، وإلا لم يحكم، وكتب الشهادة ليحكم المكتوم الكتاب الذي فيه الحكم القاضي الكاتب إليه بها، وهو الكتاب الحكمي، وهو نقل الشهادة في الحقيقة الشهادة أي الكتاب الحكمي يسمى أي القاضي سجلاً؛ لأنه سجله وكتب بحكمه: وإنما يكتب السجل حتى لا ينسى الواقعة على طول الزمان وليكون الكتاب مذكراً لها، وإلا فلا يحتاج إلى كتابة الحكم؛ لأنه قد تم بحضور الخصم نفسه أو من يقوم مقامه، إلا إذا غاب بعد الحكم عليه أو جحد الحكم، فحينئذ يكتب ليستوفي منه الحق أو لينفذ حكمه، وقوله: "وهو المدعو سجلاً"، سياق هذا الكلام يوهم انحصار إطلاق السجل على مكتوب ثبت حكمه بشهادة الشهود مع أنه لو ثبت الحكم بالإقرار وكتب بالحكم فذلك أيضاً سجل، فالمراد: أن الحجة التي فيها حكم القاضي يدعى سجلا في عرفهم. عيني فتح سجلاً : أي : أي أحكمه بالحكم، فالسجل لا يكون إلا بعد الحكم. (عيني) وإلا: أي وإن لم يكن الخصم يعني المدعى عليه حاضرا. (عيني) لم يحكم أي القاضي الكاتب بالشهادة؛ لأن الحكم على الغائب لا يجوز. (عيني معدن الحكمي نسبته إلى الحكم باعتبار المآل وليس بسجل. (عيني، فتح) نقل الشهادة: أي الكتاب الحكمي نقل الشهادة والفرق بينه وبين السجل أن السجل لا يكون إلا بعد الحكم، والكتاب الحكمي لا يكون إلا قبل الحكم، وحكم القاضي في السجل إذا وقع في مسألة مختلف فيها ليس للمكتوب إليه ولاية الرد بل عليه تنفيذه؛ لاتصال الحكم به، بخلاف الكتاب الحكمي، فإنه جاز للمكتوب إليه مخالفة رأي الكاتب ونقض حكمه
Chapter: The Judge's Letter. And he writes with his judgment, which is called a sijill; otherwise he does not judge, and he writes the testimony so that the recipient judge may judge by it; that is the kitab hukmi, which is essentially the transmission of testimony. The kitab hukmi is called (the judge's) sijill because he records it. And he writes with his judgment: the sijill is written only so that the incident is not forgotten over time and the document serves as a reminder; otherwise there is no need to write the judgment, because it has been completed in the presence of the opponent himself or his proxy, unless the opponent is absent after the judgment or denies the judgment, in which case it is written to collect the right from him or to enforce his judgment. His statement: "which is called a sijill" – this phrasing suggests that the term sijill is restricted to a document whose judgment is proven by the testimony of witnesses, whereas if the judgment is proven by confession and written, that is also a sijill. So the intended meaning is that the proof containing the judge's judgment is called a sijill in their custom. (Ayni, Fath) 'Sijill' means: he adjudicated it with the judgment, so a sijill only exists after the judgment. (Ayni) 'Otherwise': i.e., if the opponent, meaning the defendant, is not present. (Ayni) 'He does not judge': i.e., the writing judge does not judge by the testimony, because judging over an absentee is not permissible. (Ayni) 'Maden al-hukmi': its attribution to judgment is based on the outcome, and it is not a sijill. (Ayni, Fath) 'Transmission of testimony': i.e., the kitab hukmi is a transmission of testimony. The difference between it and the sijill is that the sijill only exists after the judgment, whereas the kitab hukmi only exists before the judgment. The judge's ruling in the sijill, if it occurs in a matter of disagreement, the recipient judge has no authority to reject it; rather, he must execute it, because the judgment is connected to him, unlike the kitab hukmi, for which the recipient judge may oppose the writer's opinion and overturn his judgment.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of two types of documents a judge may write: the sijill and the kitab hukmi. The author clarifies that the sijill is a record of a judgment already pronounced; it is written mainly to preserve the case for future reference, especially if the defendant becomes absent or denies the judgment. The term sijill is not restricted to cases proven by testimony; it also applies to judgments based on confession. In contrast, the kitab hukmi is a letter written before judgment, containing the testimony of witnesses, which is transmitted to another judge for him to adjudicate. The key difference is temporal: sijill after judgment, kitab hukmi before. Moreover, the recipient judge must execute the judgment in a sijill, but may review and potentially overturn the ruling based on a kitab hukmi, since the judgment has not yet been made. The passage also notes that a judge cannot pronounce a judgment in absentia; hence, the kitab hukmi merely transmits testimony, and the actual judgment is left to the recipient judge in the presence of the defendant.

Key Terms
السجل (al-sijill): A written record containing the judge's judgment, written after the judgment is pronounced, based on testimony or confession.
الكتاب الحكمي (al-kitab al-hukmi): A letter written by a judge to another judge containing testimony of witnesses, to be used as evidence for a judgment, written before the judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main difference between a sijill and a kitab hukmi?
A) A sijill is written before judgment, a kitab hukmi after.
B) A sijill is written after judgment, a kitab hukmi before.
C) A sijill is for testimony, a kitab hukmi is for ruling.
D) There is no difference.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi judicial procedure, the sijill is a post-judgment binding record, while the kitab hukmi is a pre-judgment transmission of testimony allowing the recipient judge discretion.

Conditions for a Judge's Letter and the Hanafi Dispute on Acceptance Without the Defendant
Listing conditions for a valid judge's letter and the intra-Hanafi dispute on its acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid judge's letter and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding acceptance without the defendant.

Core Idea
A judge's letter requires strict conditions; Abu Hanifa insists on the defendant's presence, while Abu Yusuf allows acceptance in absentia.

The Hanafi school has detailed rules for judicial correspondence. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq lists the conditions a judge's letter must meet and then dives into an intra-madhhab disagreement: can the recipient judge accept the letter without the defendant present? Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of how testimony (shahada) principles extend to written judgments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions of the judge's letter
List the conditions mentioned: known judge to known judge, known matter, known parties, identification by name and lineage, date, seal (disputed), witnesses testifying, reading and sealing in presence of witnesses.
These are shurut (conditions) for the letter to be acceptable.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's requirement of defendant's presence
Abu Hanifa holds that the recipient judge cannot accept the letter without the defendant present because it is like performing testimony, which requires the opponent's presence.
The analogy is qiyas jaliy: accepting the letter is akin to giving testimony.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf argues that the letter is addressed exclusively to the recipient judge, so he may accept it; the defendant's presence is only needed at the time of judgment based on the letter's content.
This is his later opinion (qawl akhir), showing a relaxation.
4
Note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the defendant must be present when the letter is presented; under Abu Yusuf's, the judge can accept the letter in the defendant's absence and then summon him for judgment.
This affects court procedure significantly.
5
Apply the conditions to a scenario
Test your understanding: if a letter lacks a date, what should the judge do? (Reject) If the seal is missing but witnesses testify, which view applies? (Abu Yusuf may accept, Abu Hanifa rejects).
Practice applying the rules to specific cases.

(عيني)] أي على من يشهدهم وهم شهود الطريق؛ لأنهم يشهدون عند الثاني، فلابد من القراءة عليهم ليعرفوا ما فيه؛ إذ لا شهادة بدون العلم، وختم الكتاب عندهم أي عند الشهود، وسلم الكتاب إليهم كي لا يتوهم التغيير، ومن شرائطه: أن يكون للكتاب أن يكتب فيه اسمه واسم أبيه وجده واسم القاضي المكتوب إليه وأبيه وجده، حتى لو أخذ بشيء منها لا يقبل الكتاب ويكتب العنوان من داخل الكتاب حتى لو كان على ظاهره لا يقبل، وقيل: هذا في عرفهم، أما في عرفنا فالعنوان يكون على الظاهر، فيعمل به، وقوله: مطلقاً. وختم عندهم عند الطرفين، قيل: إذا كان الكتاب في يد المدعي يفتى بأن الختم شرط، وإن كان في يد الشهود يفتى بأنه ليس بشرط. وعند أبي يوسف: لا يشترط شيء من ذلك، وإنما الشرط أن يشهدهم أن هذا كتابه وخاتمه، فإن شهدوا على الكتاب والختم عند القاضي المكتوب إليه كان كافيا وعنه أن الختم ليس بشرط فسهل في ذلك لما ابتلي بالقضاء وهذا قوله آخراً، وقوله الأول مثل قول أبي حنيفة، ومن الشروط أن يكون فيه التاريخ، فلو لم يكتبه لا يقبل، وقوله: "وسلم إليهم" وعمل القضاة اليوم: أنهم يسلمون إلى المدعي ويذكر اسم الشهود إن شاء، وإن شاء يكتفي بذكر شهادتهم [ رمز الحقائق: ١٢٣/٢] (فتح، عناية) فإن وصل إلخ: لما فرغ من بيان الأحكام المتعلقة بجانب القاضي الكاتب، شرع في بيان الأحكام المتعلقة بجانب المكتوب إليه، أي إذا وصل الكتاب إلى المكتوب إليه لم يقبله إلا بحضرة الخصم؛ لأن ذلك بمنزلة أداء الشهادة، وأداؤها لا يكون إلا بحضرة الخصم، فكذلك هذا بخلاف القاضي الكاتب، حيث يسمع الشهادة ويكتبها، والخصم غائب؛ لأن سماعه ليس للحكم بل للنقل، فكان جائزاً، وقال أبو يوسف يقبله من غير حضور الخصم؛ لأن الكتاب يختص بالمكتوب إليه، فكان له أن يقبله والحكم بعد ذلك يقع بما علمه من الكتاب، فاعتبر حضور الخصم عند الحكم به. (عيني، فتح، عناية) بلا خصم لأن هذا الكتاب للحكم به فلا يقبل إلا بحضور الخصم كالشهادة عليه. (عيني) أنه: أي الكتاب الذي وصل إليه. (عيني) في مجلس: أي في مجلس يصح حكمه فيه حتى لو سلم في غير ذلك المجلس لا يصح. (عيني) وفتح القاضي إلخ: هذا إذا ثبتت عدالتهم عنده بأن كان يعرفهم بالعدالة، أو وجد في الكتاب عدالتهم بأن كان القاضي الكاتب قد كتب عدالتهم أو سأل من يعرفهم من الثقات، فزكوا، وأما قبل ظهور عدالتهم فلا يحكم به، ولا يلزم الخصم؛ لأنه لا يكون حجة إلا بعد ظهور عدالتهم، وعن الخصاف أنه لا يفتحه إلا بعد ظهور عدالتهم
It is required that the letter be from a known judge to a known judge, regarding a known matter, for a known plaintiff, against a known defendant. The identification of these persons is by mentioning their names, their lineage to their father and grandfather, or their tribe. If the name of the father or grandfather is not mentioned, identification is not achieved unless the person is known by his personal name. (Miskin, Fath) Then, when it reaches the recipient judge, he looks at the writing judge, the testimony, the writer of the letter, i.e., the letter has been delivered, i.e., to the judge, i.e., the recipient judge, its seal, and he does not accept it without the opponent and witnesses. Then they testify that it is the letter of so-and-so the judge, that he delivered it to us, the writing judge, i.e., without the presence of the defendant, the witnesses in his court of judgment, and he read it to us, and sealed it, and the judge opened it, and read it to the opponent, the address, and it is the letter, the letter, the recipient judge, the letter, i.e., the defendant, the letter. And he read to them [i.e., to the witnesses whom he testifies against the letter (Ayni)] i.e., to those whom he witnesses, and they are the witnesses of the road (shuhud al-tariq), because they testify before the second judge; therefore it is necessary to read to them so that they know what is in it, since there is no testimony without knowledge. And he sealed the letter in their presence, i.e., in the presence of the witnesses, and delivered the letter to them so that change might not be suspected. Among its conditions: the letter should have written in it his name and the name of his father and grandfather, and the name of the judge to whom it is written, and his father and grandfather, such that if any of these is omitted the letter is not accepted. And the address is written on the inside of the letter; even if it is on the outside it is not accepted. It is said: this is in their custom, but in our custom the address is on the outside, so it is acted upon. His statement: absolutely. And he sealed in their presence at both ends. It is said: if the letter is in the hand of the plaintiff, the fatwa is that the seal is a condition; if it is in the hand of the witnesses, the fatwa is that it is not a condition. According to Abu Yusuf: none of that is a condition; rather the condition is that he makes them testify that this is his letter and his seal. If they testify about the letter and the seal in the presence of the recipient judge, it is sufficient. And it is reported from him that the seal is not a condition, and he made it easy in this because he was tested with the role of judge, and this is his latter opinion; his former opinion is like that of Abu Hanifa. Among the conditions is that it contain the date; if he does not write it, it is not accepted. His statement: "and delivered to them." The practice of judges today is that they deliver to the plaintiff, and he mentions the names of the witnesses if he wishes, and if he wishes he suffices with mentioning their testimony. (Fath, `Inayah) "Then when it reaches" etc.: Having finished explaining the rulings related to the side of the writing judge, he began explaining the rulings related to the side of the recipient judge. That is, when the letter reaches the recipient judge, he does not accept it except in the presence of the opponent, because that is like the performance of testimony, and its performance is only in the presence of the opponent; likewise here, unlike the writing judge, who hears the testimony and writes it while the opponent is absent, because his hearing is not for judgment but for transmission, so it is permissible. Abu Yusuf said: he accepts it without the presence of the opponent, because the letter is exclusive to the recipient judge, so he may accept it, and the judgment thereafter occurs based on what he knows from the letter; thus he considers the presence of the opponent at the time of judgment. (Ayni, Fath, `Inayah) Without an opponent, because this letter is for judgment by it, so it is not accepted except in the presence of the opponent, like testimony against him. (Ayni) 'That': i.e., the letter that reached him. (Ayni) 'In a court': i.e., in a court in which his judgment is valid; even if it is delivered in another court, it is not valid. (Ayni) 'And the judge opened' etc.: This is when their probity is established before him, either because he knows them to be upright, or because their probity is found in the letter, i.e., the writing judge wrote their probity or asked those who know them among reliable persons and they recommended them. But before their probity is apparent, he does not judge by it and it does not bind the opponent, because it is not a proof until their probity is apparent. And from al-Khassaf that he does not open it until after their probity is apparent.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage outlines the detailed conditions for a judge's letter (kitab al-qadi) and the procedure for its acceptance by the recipient judge. It begins with the conditions for the letter itself: it must be from a known judge to a known judge, concerning a known matter, with known parties. Identification must include names and lineages. When the letter reaches the recipient judge, he must not accept it without the presence of the defendant and witnesses who testify that it is indeed the sender's letter and seal. The witnesses (shuhud al-tariq) must have the letter read to them and see it sealed to prevent tampering. There is a discussion on where to write the address and the requirement of a seal, with a difference of opinion: according to Abu Hanifa, the seal is a strict condition, while Abu Yusuf's later opinion relaxes this, considering it sufficient if witnesses testify that it is the judge's letter and seal. A date is also required. The key intra-Hanafi dispute concerns whether the recipient judge can accept the letter without the defendant present. Abu Hanifa holds that acceptance requires the defendant's presence because it is analogous to the performance of testimony, which must be in the presence of the opponent. Abu Yusuf argues that the recipient judge is the addressee, so he may accept the letter and then summon the defendant for judgment. The passage also notes that the recipient judge must verify the probity of the witnesses before acting on the letter, otherwise it is not binding.

Key Terms
شهود الطريق (shuhud al-tariq): The witnesses who bring the judge's letter; they testify that it is indeed the judge's letter and seal.
الخصم (al-khasm): In this context, the defendant (al-mudda'a alayh) who is the subject of the judgment.
كتاب القاضي (kitab al-qadi): A written judgment or order from one judge to another for execution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why must the defendant be present when the recipient judge receives a kitab al-qadi?
A) Because the judge cannot write without the defendant.
B) Because it is like testimony which requires the opponent's presence.
C) Because the letter is invalid otherwise.
D) Because Abu Yusuf said so.

Key Takeaway
Conditions for a judge's letter are strict; the presence of the defendant is required per Abu Hanifa, but Abu Yusuf's view allows acceptance in absentia, with practical differences in procedure.

The Judge's Letter: Conditions and the Effect of the Writer's Death
Explains conditions for writing, sending, and receiving a judge's letter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions required for a judge's letter to be accepted and explain how the death of the writer judge affects its validity according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A judge's letter is testimony upon testimony; it requires witnesses, the opponent's presence, and is invalidated by the writer's death before reading.

In Islamic judicial procedure, a written judgment from one judge to another is not an ordinary document. It is treated as a form of testimony (shahadah 'ala shahadah) with strict conditions to ensure authenticity. This lesson unpacks those conditions and the critical ruling on what happens if the writer judge dies before the letter is executed. Understanding this helps you see how Hanafi fiqh safeguards justice through procedural rigor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the acceptance of a judge's letter by the addressee judge. The ruling is that it is subject to conditions; if they are not met, the letter is not accepted.
The overall hukm for the acceptance is that it is wajib to fulfill these conditions for validity.
2
Understand the legal nature of the letter
The letter is analogized to testimony upon testimony (shahadah 'ala shahadah). This means it requires witnesses who testify in the presence of the opponent, just like branch witnesses who report the testimony of original witnesses.
This analogy (qiyas) is the basis for many conditions.
3
Trace the dispute on invalidation by death
If the writer judge dies before the letter is read to the witnesses, the letter becomes invalid in the apparent narration (Abu Hanifa). Abu Yusuf and others hold it remains valid. Understand the proof for each side.
The dispute hinges on whether the letter is like agency (wakalah) or like a document.
4
Apply the conditions and dispute to a scenario
Given a case, determine which conditions are satisfied, and then determine the effect if the writer dies. This will clarify the practical thamarah al-khilaf.
The mufta bihi in later practice may follow Abu Yusuf for ease.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب القضاء
باب كتاب القاضي
وقرأ عليهم، وختم عندهم، وسلّم إليهم، فإن وصل إلى المكتوب إليه، نظر إلى القاضي الكاتب الشهادة الكاتب الكتاب أي الكتاب أي بلغ الكتاب أي إلى القاضي أي المكتوب إليه ختمه، ولم يقبله بلا خصم وشهود، فإن شهدوا أنه كتاب فلان القاضي سلمه إلينا القاضي الكاتب أي بلا حضور المدعى عليه الشهود في مجلس حكمه، وقرأه علينا، وختمه وفتح القاضي، وقرأه على الخصم، عنوان، وهو الكتاب الكتاب المكتوب إليه الكتاب أي المدعى عليه الكتاب وقرأ عليهم [ أي على الشهود الذين يشهدهم على الكتاب. (عيني)] أي على من يشهدهم وهم شهود الطريق؛ لأنهم يشهدون عند الثاني، فلابد من القراءة عليهم ليعرفوا ما فيه؛ إذ لا شهادة بدون العلم، وختم الكتاب عندهم أي عند الشهود، وسلم الكتاب إليهم كي لا يتوهم التغيير، ومن شرائطه: أن يكون للكتاب أن يكتب فيه اسمه واسم أبيه وجده واسم القاضي المكتوب إليه وأبيه وجده، حتى لو أخذ بشيء منها لا يقبل الكتاب ويكتب العنوان من داخل الكتاب حتى لو كان على ظاهره لا يقبل، وقيل: هذا في عرفهم، أما في عرفنا فالعنوان يكون على الظاهر، فيعمل به، وقوله: مطلقاً. وختم عندهم عند الطرفين، قيل: إذا كان الكتاب في يد المدعي يفتى بأن الختم شرط، وإن كان في يد الشهود يفتى بأنه ليس بشرط. وعند أبي يوسف: لا يشترط شيء من ذلك، وإنما الشرط أن يشهدهم أن هذا كتابه وخاتمه، فإن شهدوا على الكتاب والختم عند القاضي المكتوب إليه كان كافيا وعنه أن الختم ليس بشرط فسهل في ذلك لما ابتلي بالقضاء وهذا قوله آخراً، وقوله الأول مثل قول أبي حنيفة، ومن الشروط أن يكون فيه التاريخ، فلو لم يكتبه لا يقبل، وقوله: "وسلم إليهم" وعمل القضاة اليوم: أنهم يسلمون إلى المدعي ويذكر اسم الشهود إن شاء، وإن شاء يكتفي بذكر شهادتهم [ رمز الحقائق: ١٢٣/٢] (فتح، عناية)
فإن وصل إلخ: لما فرغ من بيان الأحكام المتعلقة بجانب القاضي الكاتب، شرع في بيان الأحكام المتعلقة بجانب المكتوب إليه، أي إذا وصل الكتاب إلى المكتوب إليه لم يقبله إلا بحضرة الخصم؛ لأن ذلك بمنزلة أداء الشهادة، وأداؤها لا يكون إلا بحضرة الخصم، فكذلك هذا بخلاف القاضي الكاتب، حيث يسمع الشهادة ويكتبها، والخصم غائب؛ لأن سماعه ليس للحكم بل للنقل، فكان جائزاً، وقال أبو يوسف يقبله من غير حضور الخصم؛ لأن الكتاب يختص بالمكتوب إليه، فكان له أن يقبله والحكم بعد ذلك يقع بما علمه من الكتاب، فاعتبر حضور الخصم عند الحكم به. (عيني، فتح، عناية)
بلا خصم لأن هذا الكتاب للحكم به فلا يقبل إلا بحضور الخصم كالشهادة عليه. (عيني) أنه: أي الكتاب الذي وصل إليه. (عيني) في مجلس: أي في مجلس يصح حكمه فيه حتى لو سلم في غير ذلك المجلس لا يصح. (عيني) وفتح القاضي إلخ: هذا إذا ثبتت عدالتهم عنده بأن كان يعرفهم بالعدالة، أو وجد في الكتاب عدالتهم بأن كان القاضي الكاتب قد كتب عدالتهم أو سأل من يعرفهم من الثقات، فزكوا، وأما قبل ظهور عدالتهم فلا يحكم به، ولا يلزم الخصم؛ لأنه لا يكون حجة إلا بعد ظهور عدالتهم، وعن الخصاف أنه لا يفتحه إلا بعد ظهور عدالتهم، =

وألزمه ما فيه، ويبطل الكتاب بموت الكاتب وعزله، وبموت المكتوب إليه المدعى عليه الكتاب القاضي ولو قالت الشهود لم يسلمه إلينا، ولم يقرأه علينا، ولم يختمه بحضرتنا، لم يعمل به، وشرط في "الذخيرة": حضور الخصم لقبول البينة بأنه كتاب ،فلان لا لقبول الكتاب، حتى لو قبله مع غيبة الخصم جاز، والأشبه: أن هذا قول أبي يوسف، فإن عنده يقبله من غير بينة، ومن يد المدعي أيضاً إذا جاء به ،وحده، ولذا لما سئل عن الإثبات، فقال: إذا شهدوا أنه كتابه، ولم يشهدوا بالختم ،وغيره قبله، وقد سئل في ذلك لما ابتلي بالقضاء، وليس الخبر كالمعاينة، ولو وجد في الكتاب ما يخالف شهادتهم، رده.
ثم لابد . من مسافة بين القاضيين حتى يجوز كتاب القاضي، واختلفوا فيها فمنهم من قال: هي معتبرة بشهادة على الشهادة، وهي مسيرة ثلاثة أيام في ظاهر الرواية وعن أبي :يوسف أنه لو كان في مكان لو غدا لأداء الشهادة لا يستطيع أن يبيت في أهله صح الإشهاد وعن محمد: أنه يجوز الشهادة على الشهادة، وإن كان الأصل صحيحاً في المصر، وذكر الكرخي في اختلاف الفقهاء": أن كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي مقبول وإن كانا في مصر واحد، فكأنهما اعتبراه بالتوكيل، وفي ظاهر الرواية اعتبر بالعجز، وفي خزانة الفقة": ويجوز كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي في المصرين أو من قاضي مصر إلى قاضي رستاق ولا يجوز من قاضي رستاق إلى قاضي رمز الحقائق: ۱۲۳/۲] (فتح) مصر.
وجب عليه ويبطل الكتاب إلخ: يعني إذا مات أو عزل الكاتب قبل وصول الكتاب إلى القاضي الثاني، أو بعد الوصول قبل أن يقرأه عليهم بطل الكتاب؛ لأنه بمنزلة الشهادة على الشهادة، وبموت الأصول قبل أداء الفروع الشهادة تبطل شهادة الفروع ، وكذا إذا أخرج الكاتب عن أهلية القضاء بجنون أو إغماء أو فسق أو ارتداد أو قذف وحد فيه، وقال أبو يوسف لا يبطل بموته، بل المكتوب إليه يقضي به، وهو قول الشافعي ومالك وأحمد في رواية، ولو مات القاضي الكاتب بعد ما قرأ الكتاب لا يبطل في ظاهر الرواية، ويحكم به المكتوب إليه؛ لأنه القضاء به بالقراءة، فلا يبطل بالموت كما لو مات الشاهد بعد أداء الشهادة قبل الحكم بها خلافاً لزفر. فإن قيل: هذه العبارة مخالفة صريحا لما في كتاب الشهادة أنه لا شهادة للفروع بلا موت أصله أو مرضه أو سفره؛ لأن جوازها للحاجة عند عجز الأصل وهو متحقق بهذه الأشياء، قلنا: لا مخالفة بينهما؛ لأن الشهادة على نوعين: أحدهما: بمعنى التوكيل وهو يبطل بموت الموكل وهو المذكور ههنا، والثاني: بمعنى الوصية بأن يقولوا للفروع: إذا متنا فاشهدوا على شهادتنا، والوصية لا تنفذ إلا بعد موت الموصي. (عيني، كفاية) وعزله : أي عزل الكاتب بعدم أهليته (مع ، ط وبموت إلخ : أي يبطل الكتاب بموت القاضي المكتوب إليه إلا إذا كتب هكذا: إلى فلان القاضي وإلى من يصل إليه من قضاة المسلمين، فيكون غير المكتوب إليه تبعاً له، فحينئذ لا يبطل بموت المكتوب إليه وقالت الثلاثة لا يبطل بموت المكتوب إليه وإن لم يقل ذلك، ويحكم القاضي الذي جاء به بعده، ولنا أن القاضي الكاتب اعتمد على علم الأول وأمانته، والقضاة يتفاوتون في الأمانة، =

Book of Judgments. Chapter on the Judge's Letter. And he read it to them, and sealed it in their presence, and handed it to them. Then when it reaches the addressee, he looks at the writer judge... the testimony... the writer judge... the letter... meaning the letter... meaning it reached the letter... meaning to the judge... meaning the addressee... its seal, and he does not accept it without the opponent and witnesses. If they testify that it is the letter of so-and-so the judge, he handed it to us... the writer judge... meaning without the presence of the defendant... the witnesses... in his court session, and he read it to us, and sealed it, and the judge opened it, and read it to the opponent... the address... and it is... the letter... the letter... the addressee... the letter... meaning the defendant... the letter... And he read it to them [i.e., to the witnesses whom he calls to witness the letter. (Ayni)] i.e., to those he calls to witness, and they are the witnesses of the road; because they testify before the second judge, so it is necessary to read it to them so that they know what is in it; for there is no testimony without knowledge. And he sealed the letter in their presence, i.e., in the presence of the witnesses, and handed the letter to them so that alteration would not be suspected. Among its conditions: that the letter should have written in it his name, his father's name, and his grandfather's name, and the name of the addressee judge, his father, and his grandfather; so that if any of these is omitted, the letter is not accepted. And the address is written on the inside of the letter; even if it is on its outer side, it is not accepted. And it is said: This is according to their custom; as for our custom, the address is on the outer side, so it is acted upon. And his statement: absolutely. And he sealed in their presence at both ends. It is said: if the letter is in the hand of the plaintiff, it is issued as a fatwa that the seal is a condition; if it is in the hand of the witnesses, it is issued as a fatwa that it is not a condition. And according to Abu Yusuf: none of that is required; rather the condition is that he makes them testify that this is his letter and his seal; if they testify to the letter and the seal before the addressee judge, it is sufficient. And from him (another narration) that the seal is not a condition; he made it easy in that because he was afflicted with judgeship, and this is his later opinion; his first opinion is like the opinion of Abu Hanifa. Among the conditions is that it contains the date; if he does not write it, it is not accepted. And his statement: "and handed it to them." And the practice of judges today is that they hand it to the plaintiff, and he mentions the names of the witnesses if he wishes, and if he wishes he suffices with mentioning their testimony. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/123] (Fath, Inayah)
"Then when it reaches etc.": When he finished explaining the rulings concerning the side of the writer judge, he began explaining the rulings concerning the side of the addressee, i.e., when the letter reaches the addressee, he does not accept it except in the presence of the opponent; because that is equivalent to performing testimony, and its performance is only done in the presence of the opponent, and so is this. Unlike the writer judge, who hears the testimony and writes it while the opponent is absent; because his hearing is not for judgment but for transmission, so it was permissible. And Abu Yusuf said: He accepts it without the presence of the opponent; because the letter is specific to the addressee, so he may accept it, and the judgment thereafter occurs based on what he learned from the letter; thus the presence of the opponent is considered at the time of judgment. (Ayni, Fath, Inayah)
Without opponent: because this letter is for judgment by it, so it is not accepted except in the presence of the opponent, like testimony against him. (Ayni) That: i.e., the letter that reached him. (Ayni) In a session: i.e., in a session in which his judgment is valid; even if it is handed over in other than that session, it is not valid. (Ayni) "And the judge opened etc.": This is if their integrity is established in his view, either because he knows them to be upright, or if he finds in the letter (a statement of) their integrity, such that the writer judge wrote their integrity or asked those who know them from trustworthy people, and they vouched for them. But before their integrity becomes apparent, he does not judge by it, and it is not binding on the opponent; because it is not a proof except after their integrity becomes apparent. And from al-Khassaf: He does not open it except after their integrity becomes apparent.
[PAGE_418] Book of Judgments, 417, Chapter on the Judge's Letter. And he enforces what is in it. And the letter becomes invalid by the death of the writer and his dismissal, and by the death of the addressee... the defendant... the letter... the judge. And if the witnesses say: He did not hand it to us, and did not read it to us, and did not seal it in our presence, it is not acted upon. And it is stipulated in al-Dhakhirah: The presence of the opponent is for accepting the evidence that it is so-and-so's letter, not for accepting the letter itself; so if he accepts it in the absence of the opponent, it is permissible. And the more likely is that this is the opinion of Abu Yusuf, because according to him he accepts it without evidence, and also from the hand of the plaintiff if he comes alone. For this reason, when he was asked about proof, he said: If they testify that it is his letter and do not testify to the seal and other things, he accepts it. And he was asked about that when he was afflicted with judgeship. And news is not like direct observation. And if in the letter there is something that contradicts their testimony, he rejects it.
Then there must be a distance between the two judges for the judge's letter to be permissible. And they differed concerning it. Among them are those who said: It is considered according to testimony upon testimony, which is a distance of three days in the apparent narration. And from Abu Yusuf: If he was in a place such that if he went out to perform testimony he could not spend the night with his family, the testimony is valid. And from Muhammad: Testimony upon testimony is permissible even if the original is valid within the city. And al-Karkhi mentioned in Ikhtilaf al-Fuqaha': The letter of a judge to a judge is acceptable even if they are in one city; it is as if they considered it by agency. And in the apparent narration, it is considered by incapacity. And in Khizanat al-Fiqh: The letter of a judge to a judge is permissible in two cities, or from a judge of a city to a judge of a rural district, and it is not permissible from a judge of a rural district to a judge of a city. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/123] (Fath)
It becomes obligatory upon him? And the letter becomes invalid etc.: Meaning if the writer dies or is dismissed before the letter reaches the second judge, or after it reaches but before he reads it to them, the letter becomes invalid; because it is like testimony upon testimony, and with the death of the original witnesses before the branch witnesses perform testimony, the testimony of the branch witnesses becomes invalid. Likewise, if the writer is removed from eligibility for judgeship by insanity, unconsciousness, immorality, apostasy, or slander for which he is flogged. And Abu Yusuf said: It does not become invalid by his death; rather the addressee judges by it. And this is the opinion of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad in one narration. And if the writer judge dies after he has read the letter, it does not become invalid in the apparent narration, and the addressee judges by it; because the judgment by it is by reading, so it is not invalidated by death, just as if a witness dies after performing testimony but before judgment is made with it, contrary to Zufar. If it is said: This statement clearly contradicts what is in the Book of Testimony, that there is no testimony for branch witnesses without the death, illness, or travel of their original; because its permissibility is due to need when the original is incapacitated, and this is realized by these things. We say: There is no contradiction between them; because testimony is of two types: one is in the sense of agency, which becomes invalid with the death of the principal, and this is what is mentioned here; the second is in the sense of bequest, such that they say to the branch witnesses: When we die, then testify to our testimony, and the bequest is not executed except after the death of the testator. (Ayni, Kifayah) And his dismissal: i.e., dismissal of the writer due to his lack of eligibility. (with, t? And by the death etc.: i.e., the letter becomes invalid by the death of the addressee judge, unless he wrote thus: "To so-and-so the judge and to whomever it reaches from the Muslim judges," then the other than the addressee is a follower of him, so then it does not become invalid by the death of the addressee. And the three (imams) said: It does not become invalid by the death of the addressee even if he did not say that, and the judge who receives it after him judges by it. And for us (the Hanafis) is that the writer judge relied on the knowledge and trustworthiness of the first, and judges differ in trustworthiness.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Judgments deals with the chapter on the judge's letter (kitab al-qadi). It explains the conditions for a judge to send a written judgment to another judge, how it should be received, and when it becomes invalid. The discussion is a commentary (sharh) on a matn (likely Kanz al-Daqa'iq) and includes differences among Hanafi imams and with other schools.

The passage begins by describing the proper procedure for writing the letter: the writer judge reads it to witnesses, seals it in their presence, and hands it to them. Upon receipt, the addressee judge does not accept it unless the opponent (defendant) is present, because it is akin to performing testimony. However, Abu Yusuf holds that the letter can be accepted without the opponent, but the judgment is only made after the opponent is present. The witnesses must testify that it is the writer judge's letter, and their integrity must be established. If the writer judge's letter lacks certain details like the names or date, it may be rejected.

The passage then discusses the invalidity of the letter. It becomes void if the writer judge dies or is dismissed before the letter reaches the addressee, or if it reaches but has not yet been read to the witnesses. Abu Yusuf differs and says it does not invalidate. If the writer dies after reading the letter to the witnesses, it remains valid. A similar dispute is mentioned regarding the death of the addressee judge. The Hanafi position distinguishes between testimony by agency (which is invalidated by death of the principal) and testimony by bequest (which is only valid after death). This resolves an apparent contradiction with the rules of branch testimony.

Finally, there is a discussion on the required distance between the two judges for the letter to be permissible. The apparent narration requires a distance of three days' travel (like testimony upon testimony), but al-Karkhi reports that some consider it permissible even within the same city based on the principle of agency. The ruling in Khizanat al-Fiqh allows it between two cities or from a city judge to a rural judge, but not vice versa.

The passage emphasizes that the judge's letter is a serious matter with strict conditions to ensure its authenticity, similar to oral testimony. The differences among the imams show a spectrum of strictness vs. ease, with Abu Yusuf generally taking a more flexible view in later practice.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي (kitab al-qadi): A written judgment or communication from one judge to another judge.
الشهادة على الشهادة (al-shahadah 'ala al-shahadah): Testimony upon testimony; branch witnesses testifying that original witnesses testified before a judge.
الخصم (al-khasm): The opponent or defendant in a case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), when does the death of the writer judge invalidate the letter?
A) If the writer dies before the letter is read to the witnesses
B) If the writer dies before the letter is written
C) If the writer dies after the letter is read but before the addressee judges
D) The writer's death never invalidates the letter

Key Takeaway
A judge's letter is treated as testimony upon testimony; its acceptance requires strict conditions, and it is invalidated by the death of the writer before the reading, unless it was already read.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
10 min
Conditions for Accepting a Judge's Letter
Ruling on when a judge's letter is accepted with witness testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for accepting a judge's letter in Hanafi fiqh and explain the intra-madhhab disagreement between Abu Yusuf and the majority regarding witness testimony.

Core Idea
A judge's letter requires full witness testimony to its sealing and reading; Abu Yusuf's relaxation was exceptional.

This passage addresses a practical question in judicial procedure: when can a judge accept a written communication (kitab al-qadi) from another judge? The general rule requires stringent verification through witnesses, but there is a notable intra-Hanafi difference. Understanding this ruling is crucial for grasping how the Hanafi school balances procedural rigor with practical necessity in qada.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling on the letter's validity
Determine the default rule: the letter is enforced when properly delivered, but it is invalidated by the writer's death, dismissal, or the addressee's death.
2
Extract the condition of witness testimony
From the statement: if witnesses do not testify that the letter was handed to them, read to them, and sealed in their presence, the letter is not acted upon. This is the majority position.
3
Note the nuance from al-Dhakhira regarding opponent presence
The condition of opponent presence is for accepting the evidence (bayyinah) that the letter is from the judge, not for accepting the letter itself. Thus, the letter can be accepted in the opponent's absence if the evidence is already given.
This shows the distinction between proof and the letter itself.
4
Identify Abu Yusuf's opposing view
Abu Yusuf accepts the letter without full testimony—if witnesses merely testify that it is the judge's letter (without sealing and reading), he accepts it. This is based on his practice when he was a judge.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Understand the basis and limitation of Abu Yusuf's view
His view is exceptional, not the general rule. The text notes that his practice was due to his affliction with the judiciary, implying that the standard rule remains the majority view.
The mufta bihi is the majority position requiring full testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألزمه ما فيه، ويبطل الكتاب بموت الكاتب وعزله، وبموت المكتوب إليه المدعى عليه الكتاب القاضي ولو قالت الشهود لم يسلمه إلينا، ولم يقرأه علينا، ولم يختمه بحضرتنا، لم يعمل به، وشرط في "الذخيرة": حضور الخصم لقبول البينة بأنه كتاب فلان لا لقبول الكتاب، حتى لو قبله مع غيبة الخصم جاز، والأشبه: أن هذا قول أبي يوسف، فإن عنده يقبله من غير بينة، ومن يد المدعي أيضاً إذا جاء به وحده، ولذا لما سئل عن الإثبات، فقال: إذا شهدوا أنه كتابه، ولم يشهدوا بالختم وغيره قبله، وقد سئل في ذلك لما ابتلي بالقضاء، وليس الخبر كالمعاينة، ولو وجد في الكتاب ما يخالف شهادتهم، رده.
And he enforces what is in it. The letter is invalidated by the death of the writer and his dismissal, and by the death of the addressee. The defendant [?]. If the witnesses say: He did not hand it to us, nor read it to us, nor seal it in our presence, it is not acted upon. In al-Dhakhira it is conditioned: the presence of the opponent for accepting the evidence that it is the letter of so-and-so, not for accepting the letter, such that if he accepted it in the opponent's absence, it is permissible. The more similar is that this is the statement of Abu Yusuf, for according to him he accepts it without evidence, and from the hand of the plaintiff as well if he brings it alone. Hence when asked about proof, he said: If they testify that it is his letter, and they do not testify to the seal and other things, he accepted it. He was asked about that when he was afflicted with the judiciary. Knowledge is not like direct observation. And if there is found in the letter something contradicting their testimony, he rejects it.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for accepting a judge's written communication (kitab al-qadi). The core ruling is that the letter is enforced when delivered properly, but it is invalidated by the writer's death, dismissal, or the addressee's death. The main point here is the requirement of witness testimony: if witnesses do not testify that the letter was handed to them, read to them, and sealed in their presence, the letter is not acted upon. The commentary from al-Dhakhira adds that the opponent must be present when evidence is given that the letter is from the judge, but this condition is for accepting the evidence, not for accepting the letter itself. Therefore, if the judge accepts the letter in the opponent's absence, it is permissible. The author notes that this view is attributed to Abu Yusuf, who accepts the letter without formal evidence (bayyinah) if the plaintiff brings it alone. Abu Yusuf was asked about proof and he said he would accept it if witnesses testify only that it is the judge's letter, even without testifying to the seal. This is based on his opinion during his tenure as chief judge. However, the general principle is that knowledge from testimony is not like direct observation, so if the letter contradicts the witnesses' testimony, it is rejected.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي (kitab al-qadi): A written communication from one judge to another regarding a legal case or judgment.
بينة (bayyinah): Formal evidence, usually testimony of two witnesses or equivalent.
ختم (khatm): Sealing; a physical mark of authentication on the letter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is a judge's letter NOT acted upon?
A) When the witnesses do not testify that the letter was handed to them, read, and sealed in their presence.
B) When the defendant is absent during the presentation of the letter.
C) When the plaintiff brings the letter alone without witnesses.
D) When the letter contains a contradiction to the judge's known rulings.

Key Takeaway
A judge's letter requires full witness testimony to its handover, reading, and sealing; Abu Yusuf's relaxation of this condition is an exceptional view not relied upon in fatwa.

The Distance Requirement for a Judge's Letter: Hanafi Perspectives
Dispute over the minimum distance for a judge's letter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on the distance required for a judge's letter and apply the mufta bihi ruling to scenarios of judicial communication.

Core Idea
The judge's letter is valid only if the witness cannot personally testify; default distance is three days, but other views relax it.

In Hanafi fiqh, a judge's written communication to another judge is a recognized method for conveying judicial decisions, but it is conditional on a distance between the two judges. This condition is derived by analogy to secondary testimony (shahadah 'ala al-shahadah). The Hanafi school has multiple views on what constitutes sufficient distance, ranging from a three-day journey to no distance requirement at all if the letter is treated as agency. This lesson explores these positions and identifies the relied-upon ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its condition
The act is sending a judge's letter to another judge. Its validity is conditional on a distance between them, as per the text: 'لابد من مسافة'. Determine the condition and its basis.
The condition is derived by analogy to secondary testimony.
2
Understand the default position (zahir al-riwayah)
The apparent narration states the distance is a journey of three days. This is the benchmark. What is the underlying reasoning?
The analogy to shahadah 'ala al-shahadah requires inability to testify in person.
3
List the alternative positions
Abu Yusuf allows a lesser distance if the witness cannot return home the same day. Muhammad allows it even if the original witness is in the city. Al-Karkhi permits it within the same city by analogy to agency.
Each scholar uses a different analogy or condition.
4
Identify the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is the three-day distance or the condition of incapacity (al-ajz) as stated in Khizanat al-Fiqh. Also note the asymmetry: city-to-village is valid, but village-to-city is not.
The mufta bihi follows the strongest analogy and the majority of later scholars.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a specific situation (e.g., distance less than three days, same city, or between city and village), determine validity according to each view and the mufta bihi.
Practice with concrete cases to internalize the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لابد من مسافة بين القاضيين حتى يجوز كتاب القاضي، واختلفوا فيها فمنهم من قال: هي معتبرة بشهادة على الشهادة، وهي مسيرة ثلاثة أيام في ظاهر الرواية وعن أبي يوسف أنه لو كان في مكان لو غدا لأداء الشهادة لا يستطيع أن يبيت في أهله صح الإشهاد وعن محمد: أنه يجوز الشهادة على الشهادة، وإن كان الأصل صحيحاً في المصر، وذكر الكرخي في اختلاف الفقهاء: أن كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي مقبول وإن كانا في مصر واحد، فكأنهما اعتبراه بالتوكيل، وفي ظاهر الرواية اعتبر بالعجز، وفي خزانة الفقه: ويجوز كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي في المصرين أو من قاضي مصر إلى قاضي رستاق ولا يجوز من قاضي رستاق إلى قاضي مصر.
Then there must be a distance between the two judges for the judge's letter to be permissible. They differed about it. Some said: It is considered like testimony on testimony, which is a journey of three days according to the apparent narration. From Abu Yusuf: If he were in a place where if he went early to give testimony he could not spend the night with his family, then the attestation is valid. From Muhammad: That testimony on testimony is permissible even if the original witness is present in the city. Al-Karkhi mentioned in 'Ikhtilaf al-Fuqaha': That the judge's letter to a judge is accepted even if they are in one city, as if they considered it like agency. In the apparent narration, it is considered by incapacity. In Khizanat al-Fiqh: The judge's letter to a judge is permissible in two cities or from a city judge to a village judge, but not from a village judge to a city judge.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage addresses the spatial requirement for the validity of a judge's written communication. The letter is treated similarly to testimony on testimony (shahadah 'ala al-shahadah), which requires a distance that prevents the original witness from personally testifying. The default position in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) is a distance of three days' journey. However, there are varying opinions: Abu Yusuf considers the condition fulfilled if the witness cannot go to testify and return home the same day. Muhammad al-Shaybani allows testimony on testimony even if the original witness is present in the same city, implying no distance requirement. Al-Karkhi reports an opinion that the letter is accepted even within the same city, treating it like agency (tawkeel). Khizanat al-Fiqh adds a distinction: a letter from a city judge to a village judge is valid, but not vice versa. The passage shows that the Hanafi school has multiple approaches to this issue.

Key Terms
شهادة على الشهادة (shahadah 'ala al-shahadah): Testimony by secondary witnesses who heard from the original witnesses; used as analogy for the judge's letter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A judge's letter from a city judge to a village judge.
B) A judge's letter from a village judge to a city judge.
C) A judge's letter between two cities at a distance of three days.
D) A judge's letter between two cities at a distance of one day.

Key Takeaway
The judge's letter is analogous to secondary testimony; the relied-upon condition is a distance of three days or the witness's incapacity to testify in person.

Invalidation of a Judge's Letter by Death or Dismissal
Ruling on when a judge's letter becomes void due to death or dismissal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a judge's letter becomes void, explain the qiyas to testimony, and describe the intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A judge's letter is like testimony: death of the writer before reading voids it, but Abu Yusuf disagrees.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq examines a key procedural issue in qada: the effect of the writer judge's death or dismissal on his written judgment. The ruling is analogized to testimony on testimony, and a significant intra-Hanafi dispute emerges with Abu Yusuf. Let's dissect the ruling and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Primary Ruling
Locate the matn's statement: the letter is invalidated by the writer's death, dismissal, and the addressee's death. Recognize that these are three separate triggers.
The matn is concise; the commentary expands.
2
Understand the Analogy to Testimony
The letter is analogized to testimony on testimony (shahadah 'ala al-shahadah). The writer is like the original witness, and the addressee judge is like the branch witness. Death of the original witness before the branch testifies voids the branch testimony.
This is a qiyas jaliy: the illa is dependency on the original's existence.
3
Recognize the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that the letter is not invalidated by the writer's death. He is joined by the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools in one narration. The majority (zahir al-riwayah) follows Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani that it is invalidated.
The tarjih is on the majority view for fatwa.
4
Note the Exception for After Reading
If the writer dies after reading the letter to the addressee, the letter is not invalidated in the apparent narration, because the judgment is effectuated by the reading.
This is analogous to a witness completing testimony before death.
5
Analyze the Addressee's Death
The matn says the letter is invalidated by the addressee's death, but the three eponyms (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) are reported to hold that it is not invalidated, unless the writer specifically addressed only that individual. The majority opinion allows for a general address to preserve the letter.
This shows the nuance: the school's view is not monolithic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبطل الكتاب بموت الكاتب وعزله، وبموت المكتوب إليه المدعى عليه الكتاب القاضي ولو قالت الشهود لم يسلمه إلينا، ولم يقرأه علينا، ولم يختمه بحضرتنا، لم يعمل به... ويبطل الكتاب إلخ: يعني إذا مات أو عزل الكاتب قبل وصول الكتاب إلى القاضي الثاني، أو بعد الوصول قبل أن يقرأه عليهم بطل الكتاب؛ لأنه بمنزلة الشهادة على الشهادة، وبموت الأصول قبل أداء الفروع الشهادة تبطل شهادة الفروع، وكذا إذا أخرج الكاتب عن أهلية القضاء بجنون أو إغماء أو فسق أو ارتداد أو قذف وحد فيه، وقال أبو يوسف لا يبطل بموته، بل المكتوب إليه يقضي به، وهو قول الشافعي ومالك وأحمد في رواية، ولو مات القاضي الكاتب بعد ما قرأ الكتاب لا يبطل في ظاهر الرواية، ويحكم به المكتوب إليه؛ لأنه القضاء به بالقراءة، فلا يبطل بالموت كما لو مات الشاهد بعد أداء الشهادة قبل الحكم بها خلافاً لزفر. (عيني، كفاية) وعزله : أي عزل الكاتب بعدم أهليته (مع، ط وبموت إلخ : أي يبطل الكتاب بموت القاضي المكتوب إليه إلا إذا كتب هكذا: إلى فلان القاضي وإلى من يصل إليه من قضاة المسلمين، فيكون غير المكتوب إليه تبعاً له، فحينئذ لا يبطل بموت المكتوب إليه وقالت الثلاثة لا يبطل بموت المكتوب إليه وإن لم يقل ذلك، ويحكم القاضي الذي جاء به بعده، ولنا أن القاضي الكاتب اعتمد على علم الأول وأمانته، والقضاة يتفاوتون في الأمانة، إلا إذا كتب بعد اسمه وإلى كل من يصل إليه من قضاة المسلمين، لا بموت الخصم، أي القاضي الكاتب القاضي تقضي المرأة في غير حدّ وقود، ولا يستخلف قاض إلا أن يفوض إليه ذلك.
And the letter is invalidated by the death of the writer and his dismissal, and by the death of the addressee... The commentary: It means if the writer dies or is dismissed before the letter reaches the second judge, or after it reaches but before he reads it to them, the letter is invalidated, because it is like testimony on testimony. By the death of the original witnesses before the branch witnesses perform the testimony, the branch testimony becomes void. Similarly if the writer leaves the qualification for judiciary due to insanity, unconsciousness, sin, apostasy, or slander and is punished for it. Abu Yusuf said: It is not invalidated by his death; rather the addressee judges by it. This is the statement of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad in one narration. If the writer judge dies after reading the letter, it is not invalidated in the apparent narration, and the addressee judges by it, because the judgment is effected by the reading, so it is not invalidated by death, as when a witness dies after giving testimony before judgment, contrary to Zufar. (Al-'Ayni, Kifayah) 'And his dismissal': i.e., the dismissal of the writer due to lack of qualification. 'And by the death etc.': i.e., the letter is invalidated by the death of the addressee judge unless he wrote thus: 'To so-and-so judge and to whoever reaches him from among the Muslim judges', then the other is subsidiary to him; then it is not invalidated by the death of the addressee. The three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) said: It is not invalidated by the death of the addressee even if he did not say that, and the judge who receives it after him judges by it. Our argument is that the writer judge relied on the knowledge and trustworthiness of the first, and judges differ in trustworthiness, except if he wrote after his name 'and to everyone who reaches him from among the Muslim judges'. Not invalidated by the death of the litigant...
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the invalidation of the judge's letter. The matn states that the letter is invalidated by the writer's death or dismissal and by the death of the addressee. The commentary explains that if the writer dies or is dismissed before the letter reaches the second judge, or even after delivery but before being read, the letter is void, analogized to testimony on testimony where the death of the original witness nullifies the branch testimony. Any loss of qualification (insanity, apostasy, etc.) also invalidates. Abu Yusuf dissents: the letter is not invalidated by the writer's death; rather, the addressee should still judge by it—a view shared by al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad. However, if the writer dies after reading the letter, the judgment stands in the apparent narration, as reading effectuates the judgment like a witness completing testimony. Regarding the addressee's death, the letter is invalidated unless the writer specified a general address ('to so-and-so and to whoever reaches him')—then others can act. Interestingly, the three eponyms (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) are reported to say that the letter is not invalidated by the addressee's death even without such a clause, but the writer's reliance on the addressee's specific qualities is cited as reasoning for the default invalidation. Death of the litigant does not invalidate, as heirs or agents take over.

Key Terms
الأصول (al-usul): Original witnesses who gave testimony that is being transmitted via the letter.
الكتاب (al-kitab): The written judgment letter sent by a judge to another judge.
الشهادة على الشهادة (al-shahadah 'ala al-shahadah): Testimony on testimony, where branch witnesses testify to the original witnesses' testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The letter is invalidated.
B) The second judge can still act on it.
C) It depends on the nature of the case.
D) Only if the addressee accepts it.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a judge's letter hinges on the writer's continued authority until the letter is read; death or dismissal before reading voids it, but after reading it remains effective, with a legitimate intra-Hanafi dispute on the matter.

Women as Judges in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on women judges with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on women judges and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A woman may judge in all cases except hudud and qisas, by analogy to her testimony.

This passage addresses the capacity of women to serve as judges, a matter of ikhtilaf between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. The Hanafi position permits her judgment in financial and civil matters, while Shafi'i prohibits it entirely. The discussion includes evidence from testimony, the hadith about women's leadership, and the possibility of ratification in hudud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi ruling
Extract the general principle: a woman may judge in all cases except hadd and qisas (retaliation). Note the analogy to testimony: her testimony is accepted in non-hudud/property matters, so her judgment is likewise accepted.
2
Locate the opposing view and its proof
Al-Shafi'i prohibits women judges based on the hadith 'A people who entrust their affairs to a woman will never succeed' and a perceived deficiency in intellect.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal: the hadith blames the appointers, not the woman's rulings; deficiency in intellect does not remove all legal capacity (e.g., she can be a witness, supervisor of waqf, guardian).
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi variation
Al-'Attabi and al-Nasafi allow a woman's judgment in hudud/qisas if ratified or based on mixed testimony, because the judgment itself is a matter of ijtihad.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under Hanafi, a woman's property judgment is valid; under Shafi'i, it is invalid. Under Hanafi, if she judges in hudud and a subsequent judge ratifies, it is effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقضي المرأة في غير حدّ وقود، ولا يستخلف قاض إلا أن يفوض إليه ذلك، تقضي المرأة إلخ: لما فرغ من بيان كتاب القاضي شرع في غيره فقال: وتقضي إلخ. (مسكين) غير حد وقود [ أي قصاص؛ لأن القضاء كالشهادة، وشهادتها جائزة في غيرهما فكذا يجوز قضاؤها في غيرهما. (عيني)] لما مر من أهل قضاء أهل الشهادة وشهادة المرأة جائزة في غير الحد والقصاص، فكذا قضاؤها إلا أن موليها آثم لخبر خاب قوم ولوا أمرهم امرأة، وعن الشافعي أنه لا يجوز أن تولي المرأة؛ لقصور عقلها بإخبار الشارع، قلنا: نقصان عقلها لا يفيد سلب أهليتها بالكلية ألا ترى أنها تصلح شاهدة وناظرة في الأوقاف ووصية على اليتامى وقال الإمام العتابي امرأة قلدت القضاء، وقضت في الأموال صح؛ لأنها تصلح شاهدة في باب الأموال، فتصلح قاضية فيها، ولو قضت بالحدود والقصاص، وأمضاه قاض آخر يرى جوازه، نفذ بالإجماع؛ لأن نفس القضاء مجتهد فيه، فإن شريحاً كان يجوز شهادة النساء مع رجل في الحدود والقصاص، وقال الشيخ أبو الحسين النسفي: ولو قضى القاضي في الحدود والقصاص بشهادة رجل وامرأتين نفذ قضاؤه، وليس لغيره إبطاله؛ لأنه قضى في فصل مجتهد فيه، وليس نفس القضاء هنا مختلفاً فيه. [رمز الحقائق: ١٢٥/٢] (فتح) ولا يستخلف : [ أي لا يجوز الاستخلاف على القضاء. (ط، عيني)] ولو بعذر؛ لأنه مفوض إليه القضاء دون التقليد، فصار كالوكيل بالبيع أو النكاح ليس له أن يوكل إلا بإذن الموكل أو التفويض، فلو استخلف من لم يفوض إليه ذلك، فقضى الثاني بمحضر من الأول، أو بغير محضره، إلا أنه أجازه، جاز كالوكيل إذا وكل مع عدم الإذن بشرط أن يكون الثاني أهلاً للقضاء لا رقيقاً ولا محدوداً في قذف ولا كافراً، فإن لم يكن أهلاً لم يجز.
A woman judges in other than [cases of] hadd and retaliation. A judge may not delegate unless that is delegated to him. 'A woman judges etc.': When he finished explaining the judge's letter, he began with other matters and said: 'She judges etc.' (Miskin) 'Other than hadd and retaliation': i.e., qisas; because judgment is like testimony, and her testimony is permissible in other than these two, so too her judgment is permissible in other than these two. (Al-'Ayni) As previously mentioned that the people of judgment are the people of testimony, and a woman's testimony is permissible in other than hadd and qisas, so too her judgment. However, her guardian is sinful due to the hadith: 'A people who entrust their affairs to a woman will never succeed.' Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible to appoint a woman due to defect in her intellect as reported by the Lawgiver. We respond: The defect in her intellect does not remove her capacity entirely; do you not see that she is suitable as a witness, a supervisor of endowments, and a guardian of orphans? Imam al-'Attabi said: A woman who is appointed to the judiciary and judges in property matters is valid because she is suitable as a witness in property matters, so she is suitable as a judge therein. If she judges in hadd and qisas, and another judge who deems it permissible ratifies it, it is executed by consensus, because the judgment itself is a matter of ijtihad. Indeed, Shurayh used to allow the testimony of women with a man in hadd and qisas. Shaykh Abu al-Husayn al-Nasafi said: If a judge judges in hadd and qisas based on the testimony of one man and two women, his judgment is executed, and no one else may nullify it, because he judged in a matter of ijtihad, and the judgment itself is not disputed here. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/125, Fath) 'And he may not delegate': i.e., it is not permissible to delegate the judiciary. (T, 'Ayni) Even if there is an excuse, because he is entrusted with judging, not with the delegation, so he is like an agent for sale or marriage who may not sub-agency without the principal's permission or delegation. If he delegates someone to whom that was not delegated, and the second judges in the presence of the first, or in his absence but he permits it, it is permissible, like an agent who sub-agents without permission, provided the second is qualified for the judiciary: not a slave, nor one punished for slander, nor a disbeliever. If he is not qualified, it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues: women judges and the delegation of judicial authority. On women judges, the ruling is that a woman may judge in all cases except those involving hadd penalties (e.g., drinking, theft, adultery) and qisas (retaliation). This is by analogy to her testimony, which is accepted in financial and other non-hudud matters. However, her guardian is sinful for appointing her due to the hadith warning against entrusting affairs to women. Al-Shafi'i's opposition is refuted by noting that the hadith indicates a deficiency in intellect but does not negate legal capacity entirely, as women can be witnesses, supervisors of waqf, and guardians of orphans. If a woman does judge in hadd/qisas and a subsequent judge ratifies it, it is effective because such rulings are subject to ijtihad. The example of Shurayh allowing mixed gender testimony in hadd shows the scope of ijtihad. On delegation, a judge may not appoint another judge on his own authority unless he is specifically delegated the power to do so. If he does delegate without authorization, the second judge's rulings are only valid if the first judge was present or later ratifies them, and the second judge must be qualified (free, not punished for slander, Muslim). This is analogous to a principal agent's inability to sub-agent without permission.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): Fixed penalties prescribed by Islamic law for specific crimes (theft, zina, qadhf, etc.).
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation or equitable retribution, applicable in cases of murder or bodily harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in the passage, in what types of cases may a woman judge?
A) Only in property cases.
B) In all cases except hadd and qisas.
C) In all cases including hadd and qisas.
D) She cannot be a judge at all.

Key Takeaway
A woman may judge in all cases except hudud and qisas, by analogy to her testimony, but her appointment is blameworthy.

6 min
Delegation of Judicial Authority (Istikhlaf)
Ruling on delegation of judiciary with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on delegation of judiciary and identify the conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
A judge cannot delegate his authority unless explicitly authorized; unauthorized rulings may be ratified if the delegate is qualified.

This passage explains the limits of a judge's power to appoint another judge. The general rule is that a judge may not delegate unless he has been specifically delegated the authority to do so. If he delegates without authorization, the second judge's rulings may still be valid if ratified by the first or if the first was present, provided the second judge is qualified.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule on delegation
The passage states: 'A judge may not delegate.' This is the default: a judge cannot appoint another judge on his own.
Exception: 'unless that is delegated to him' – explicit permission from the appointing authority.
2
Identify the analogy to agency
The judge is like an agent for sale or marriage; an agent cannot sub-agent without the principal's permission.
3
Examine the conditions for ratification
If a judge delegates without authorization but the second judge rules either in his presence or with his subsequent approval, the ruling is valid.
The first judge's presence or ratification implies consent.
4
List the qualifications of the delegate
For the delegation to be effective (if ratified), the second judge must be: free, not previously punished for slander, Muslim. If not qualified, the delegation is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقضي المرأة في غير حدّ وقود، ولا يستخلف قاض إلا أن يفوض إليه ذلك، تقضي المرأة إلخ: لما فرغ من بيان كتاب القاضي شرع في غيره فقال: وتقضي إلخ. (مسكين) غير حد وقود [ أي قصاص؛ لأن القضاء كالشهادة، وشهادتها جائزة في غيرهما فكذا يجوز قضاؤها في غيرهما. (عيني)] لما مر من أهل قضاء أهل الشهادة وشهادة المرأة جائزة في غير الحد والقصاص، فكذا قضاؤها إلا أن موليها آثم لخبر خاب قوم ولوا أمرهم امرأة، وعن الشافعي أنه لا يجوز أن تولي المرأة؛ لقصور عقلها بإخبار الشارع، قلنا: نقصان عقلها لا يفيد سلب أهليتها بالكلية ألا ترى أنها تصلح شاهدة وناظرة في الأوقاف ووصية على اليتامى وقال الإمام العتابي امرأة قلدت القضاء، وقضت في الأموال صح؛ لأنها تصلح شاهدة في باب الأموال، فتصلح قاضية فيها، ولو قضت بالحدود والقصاص، وأمضاه قاض آخر يرى جوازه، نفذ بالإجماع؛ لأن نفس القضاء مجتهد فيه، فإن شريحاً كان يجوز شهادة النساء مع رجل في الحدود والقصاص، وقال الشيخ أبو الحسين النسفي: ولو قضى القاضي في الحدود والقصاص بشهادة رجل وامرأتين نفذ قضاؤه، وليس لغيره إبطاله؛ لأنه قضى في فصل مجتهد فيه، وليس نفس القضاء هنا مختلفاً فيه. [رمز الحقائق: ١٢٥/٢] (فتح) ولا يستخلف : [ أي لا يجوز الاستخلاف على القضاء. (ط، عيني)] ولو بعذر؛ لأنه مفوض إليه القضاء دون التقليد، فصار كالوكيل بالبيع أو النكاح ليس له أن يوكل إلا بإذن الموكل أو التفويض، فلو استخلف من لم يفوض إليه ذلك، فقضى الثاني بمحضر من الأول، أو بغير محضره، إلا أنه أجازه، جاز كالوكيل إذا وكل مع عدم الإذن بشرط أن يكون الثاني أهلاً للقضاء لا رقيقاً ولا محدوداً في قذف ولا كافراً، فإن لم يكن أهلاً لم يجز.
A woman judges in other than [cases of] hadd and retaliation. A judge may not delegate unless that is delegated to him. 'A woman judges etc.': When he finished explaining the judge's letter, he began with other matters and said: 'She judges etc.' (Miskin) 'Other than hadd and retaliation': i.e., qisas; because judgment is like testimony, and her testimony is permissible in other than these two, so too her judgment is permissible in other than these two. (Al-'Ayni) As previously mentioned that the people of judgment are the people of testimony, and a woman's testimony is permissible in other than hadd and qisas, so too her judgment. However, her guardian is sinful due to the hadith: 'A people who entrust their affairs to a woman will never succeed.' Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible to appoint a woman due to defect in her intellect as reported by the Lawgiver. We respond: The defect in her intellect does not remove her capacity entirely; do you not see that she is suitable as a witness, a supervisor of endowments, and a guardian of orphans? Imam al-'Attabi said: A woman who is appointed to the judiciary and judges in property matters is valid because she is suitable as a witness in property matters, so she is suitable as a judge therein. If she judges in hadd and qisas, and another judge who deems it permissible ratifies it, it is executed by consensus, because the judgment itself is a matter of ijtihad. Indeed, Shurayh used to allow the testimony of women with a man in hadd and qisas. Shaykh Abu al-Husayn al-Nasafi said: If a judge judges in hadd and qisas based on the testimony of one man and two women, his judgment is executed, and no one else may nullify it, because he judged in a matter of ijtihad, and the judgment itself is not disputed here. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/125, Fath) 'And he may not delegate': i.e., it is not permissible to delegate the judiciary. (T, 'Ayni) Even if there is an excuse, because he is entrusted with judging, not with the delegation, so he is like an agent for sale or marriage who may not sub-agency without the principal's permission or delegation. If he delegates someone to whom that was not delegated, and the second judges in the presence of the first, or in his absence but he permits it, it is permissible, like an agent who sub-agents without permission, provided the second is qualified for the judiciary: not a slave, nor one punished for slander, nor a disbeliever. If he is not qualified, it is not permissible.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues: women judges and the delegation of judicial authority. On women judges, the ruling is that a woman may judge in all cases except those involving hadd penalties (e.g., drinking, theft, adultery) and qisas (retaliation). This is by analogy to her testimony, which is accepted in financial and other non-hudud matters. However, her guardian is sinful for appointing her due to the hadith warning against entrusting affairs to women. Al-Shafi'i's opposition is refuted by noting that the hadith indicates a deficiency in intellect but does not negate legal capacity entirely, as women can be witnesses, supervisors of waqf, and guardians of orphans. If a woman does judge in hadd/qisas and a subsequent judge ratifies it, it is effective because such rulings are subject to ijtihad. The example of Shurayh allowing mixed gender testimony in hadd shows the scope of ijtihad. On delegation, a judge may not appoint another judge on his own authority unless he is specifically delegated the power to do so. If he does delegate without authorization, the second judge's rulings are only valid if the first judge was present or later ratifies them, and the second judge must be qualified (free, not punished for slander, Muslim). This is analogous to a principal agent's inability to sub-agent without permission.

Key Terms
استخلاف (istikhlaf): Delegation of judicial authority by a judge to another person.
وكيل (wakil): Agent or representative in a legal matter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition can a judge delegate his authority?
A) In any case if he has a valid excuse.
B) Only if his ruling is later ratified by the ruler.
C) Only if he has been specifically authorized to delegate.
D) Only if the delegate is a Muslim free man.

Key Takeaway
A judge cannot delegate his authority unless explicitly authorized; unauthorized rulings may be ratified if the delegate is qualified.
