Revocation, Ratification, and Judgment for Relatives in Arbitration
Explains revocation before judgment, qadi's ratification, and nullity of judgment for close relatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules for revocation of an arbitrator, the qadi's role in ratification, and explain why a judgment for close relatives is void.

Core Idea
An arbitrator's judgment binds the litigants but may be annulled by the qadi if it contradicts his school; judgments for close relatives are null due to suspicion of bias.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses key procedural rules in arbitration (tahkim). It covers when the parties can revoke the arbitrator, how a qadi can either ratify or annul the arbitrator's judgment based on school alignment, and the invalidity of judgments for close relatives due to tuhmah. Understanding these rules is essential for anyone involved in dispute resolution or Islamic court procedures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties' right to revoke
According to the text, each litigant may revoke the arbitrator (muhakkim) before he pronounces judgment. Once judgment is given, it becomes binding and cannot be revoked by them. Reason: the arbitrator is their delegate until he judges.
This right is lost after the judgment is delivered.
2
Analyze the effect of the arbitrator's judgment
The judgment binds the litigants because it arises from a legitimate (shar'i) authority, similar to a qadi's judgment. Dismissal after judgment does not invalidate it. The judgment also extends to third parties in certain contexts.
E.g., a ruling on a defective item sold binds the buyer and seller.
3
Understand the qadi's ratification and annulment powers
When the arbitrator's judgment is presented to a qadi, the qadi enforces it if it agrees with his own madhhab (school). If it contradicts, the qadi may annul it. This is unlike a regular qadi's judgment, which cannot be annulled by another qadi merely for school differences unless it violates clear evidence.
The qadi annuls by not acting on it and giving his own decision.
4
Examine the rule for close relatives (tuhmah)
A judgment (whether by arbitrator or qadi) in favor of one's parents, child, or spouse is invalid due to suspicion of bias. However, a judgment against them is valid because no such suspicion exists. This mirrors the rule for testimony.
The reasoning: eligibility for testimony is a condition for judgeship; testimony for these relatives is not accepted.
5
Compare with the opposing view of Ibn Abi Layla
Ibn Abi Layla held that the arbitrator is like a deputy of the imam, making his judgment binding on the qadi unless it contradicts primary evidence. The majority (mufta bihi) view, however, gives the qadi discretion to annul if it goes against his school.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that under the majority, a qadi can reject a judgment from another region if it conflicts with his school.

ولكل المحكمين أن يرجع قبل حكمه، فإن حكم لزمهما، وأمضى القاضي حكمه إن وافق مذهبه، وإلا أبطله وبطل حكمه لأبويه وولده وزوجته كحكم القاضي، لأنه لا فائدة في نقضه بخلاف حكمه عليهم. ولكل المحكمين بتشديد الكاف وكسرها على صيغة التثنية أي لكل واحد منهما. (عيني) قبل حكمه أي قبل حكم المحكم؛ لأنه مقلد من جهتهما فكان لهما عزله قبل أن يحكم بينهما. (عيني) لزمهما لان حكمه صدر عن ولاية شرعية كالقاضي إذا حكم لزم، ثم بالعزل لا يبطل حكمه، فكذا هذا، وأشار إلى أنه يتعدى حكمه إلى غيرهما فلو حكماه في عيب مبيع فقضى برده، ليس للبائع أن يرده على بائعه الأول إلا برضاه. رمز الحقائق: ١٢٩/٢] (فتح) وأمضى القاضي حكمه أي إذا رفع حكم المحكم إلى القاضي نفذه إن كان موافقا لمذهبه؛ لأنه لا فائدة في نقضه، ثم فائدة هذا الإمضاء: أن لا يكون للقاضي الآخر الذي يرى خلافه نقضه إذا رفع إليه. ولو أخبر هذا المحكم بإقرار أحد الخصمين أو بعدالة الشهود وهما على حالهما، يقبل قوله؛ لأن الولاية قائمة وإن أخبر بالحكم بعد العزل لا يقبل؛ لانقضاء الولاية. وإلا أبطله أي وإن لم يوافق مذهبه بأن حكم بقول أبي يوسف مثلا، والقاضي يرى قول أبي حنيفة أبطله إن شاء؛ لأن حكمه لا يلزم القاضي؛ لعدم التحكيم من جهته، بخلاف ما إذا رفع إليه حكم حاكم حيث لا يبطله وإن خالف مذهبه، إلا أن يخالف الأدلة الشرعية كما مر وقال ابن أبي ليلى هو بمنزلة المولى من جهة الإمام حتى لا يكون لأحد أن ينقض حكمه ما لم يخالف الدليل الشرعي، ومعنى "أبطله" أي لم يعمل به، وقضى برأيه، ولا يشترط أن يقول : أبطلت حكمه رمز الحقائق: ١٢٩/٢] (فتح) وبطل حكمه إلخ لأن أهلية الشهادة شرط للقضاء والشهادة لهؤلاء غير مقبولة، فكذلك الحكم سواء كان من المحكم أو من القاضي إلا إذا ورد عليه كتاب قاض لمن لا تقبل شهادته له فيجوز قضاؤه لهم. (فتح، عناية) كحكم القاضي: أي كما يبطل حكم القاضي لهؤلاء للتهمة ( عيني عليهم أي على أبويه وولده وزوجته حيث يصح لعدم التهمة كالشهادة. (ط، عيني)
And each of the two persons (who appointed the arbitrator) can revoke before his judgment; if he judges, it binds them. The qadi ratifies his judgment if it agrees with his school; otherwise he annuls it. His judgment is invalid for his parents, child, and wife, like the judgment of a judge, because there is no benefit in revoking it unlike his judgment against them. 'For each of the two (muhakkimayn)' with tashdid on kaf and kasra, dual form, meaning each one of them (the two litigants). (Ayni) 'Before his judgment' means before the judgment of the arbitrator, because he is delegated by them, so they have the right to dismiss him before he judges between them. (Ayni) 'It binds them' because his judgment arises from a legitimate authority like a judge; if a judge gives a judgment it is binding, and dismissal does not invalidate it. Similarly here. And it is indicated that his judgment extends to others: if they appointed him regarding a defect in a sold item and he ruled for its return, the seller cannot return it to his own seller without his consent. (Fath) 'The qadi ratifies his judgment' means that if the arbitrator's judgment is brought to the qadi, he enforces it if it agrees with his school, because there is no benefit in annulling it. The benefit of this ratification is that another qadi who holds the opposite view cannot annul it if it is brought to him. And if this arbitrator reports the confession of one of the litigants or the justice of the witnesses while they are still in that state, his statement is accepted because the authority still exists. But if he reports the judgment after dismissal, it is not accepted because the authority has ended. 'Otherwise he annuls it' means if it does not agree with his school, for example, if he judged by the opinion of Abu Yusuf and the qadi follows Abu Hanifa, he annuls it if he wishes, because his judgment is not binding on the qadi since the qadi was not a party to the arbitration. Unlike when a judge's judgment is brought to him, he does not annul it even if it contradicts his school, unless it contradicts the primary evidences. Ibn Abi Layla said: He is like the deputy of the imam, such that no one can annul his judgment unless it contradicts a legal evidence. The meaning of 'annuls it' is that he does not act upon it and gives his own decision; it is not necessary to say 'I annul his judgment.' (Fath) 'His judgment is invalid...' because eligibility for testimony is a condition for judgeship, and testimony for these relatives is not accepted; similarly, the judgment, whether by an arbitrator or a judge, is invalid for them unless a letter from a judge arrives for someone whose testimony is not accepted for him, then it is permissible for him to judge for them. (Fath, Inayah) 'Like the judgment of a judge' meaning just as the judgment of a judge is invalid for these due to suspicion (tuhmah). (Ayni) 'Against them' means for his parents, child, and wife, where it is valid due to absence of suspicion, like testimony. (T, Ayni)
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on arbitration. It covers three main topics: (1) Revocation: Each of the two litigants may revoke the arbitrator (muhakkim) before he pronounces judgment, because he is their delegate. Once he gives judgment, it becomes binding and cannot be revoked by them. (2) Ratification by the qadi: When the arbitrator's judgment is presented to a qadi, the qadi enforces it if it agrees with his own madhhab; if it contradicts, the qadi may annul it (at his discretion). However, this is unlike the judgment of a regular judge (qadi) which cannot be annulled by another qadi merely for madhhab difference, unless it contradicts clear legal evidence. The passage also notes that the arbitrator's report of a confession or witness credibility is accepted as long as his authority continues, but not after revocation. (3) Judgment for/against relatives: The arbitrator's (or judge's) judgment in favor of his parents, children, or spouse is invalid due to suspicion (tuhmah) of bias. This mirrors the rule that testimony for these relatives is not accepted. However, if the judgment is against them (i.e., ruling against one's relative), it is valid because there is no suspicion of bias in that direction. The passage cites Ibn Abi Layla's opinion that the arbitrator is like a deputy of the imam, which would make his judgment more binding, but the Hanafi position as stated is that the qadi can annul if it disagrees with his school.

Key Terms
يرجع (yarjiʿ): To revoke the arbitrator before his judgment.
أمضى القاضي حكمه (amḍā al-qāḍī ḥukmahu): The qadi ratifies/enforces the arbitrator's judgment.
التهمة (al-tuhmah): Suspicion of bias that invalidates judgment for close relatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) As soon as they appoint him
B) After he has given his judgment
C) Only after the qadi ratifies it
D) Never, they can always revoke it

Key Takeaway
An arbitrator's judgment binds the parties once given, but the qadi may annul it if it opposes his school; judgments for close relatives are void due to tuhmah.

Arbitration (Tahkim): Binding Effect and Judicial Review
Explains rulings on arbitration and its enforcement by the judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the binding effect of an arbitrator's judgment and evaluate the judge's authority to annul it based on madhhab disagreement.

Core Idea
An arbitrator's judgment binds the litigants but not the judge—the judge may annul it if it contradicts his own madhhab.

Arbitration (tahkim) is a private means of dispute resolution recognized in Hanafi fiqh. When two litigants agree to appoint an arbitrator, his judgment becomes binding on them, but what happens when that judgment is presented to a state judge? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains that the judge is not bound by the arbitrator's ruling and may annul it if it conflicts with his own madhhab, a key practical difference that litigants and judges alike must understand.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is arbitration (tahkim). The primary ruling is that the arbitrator's judgment binds the litigants who appointed him.
The binding effect is analogous to a judge's judgment.
2
Locate the proof
The text uses analogy (qiyas) to a judge: 'his judgment is based on a valid authority, like a judge: if he judges, it is binding.' This is the dalil for the binding effect.
The proof for the judge's authority to annul is that the arbitrator is not appointed by the judge.
3
Note the exception regarding relatives
The arbitrator's judgment is invalid for his parents, child, and wife due to tuhma (suspicion of bias), just like a judge's judgment.
This exception applies to both arbitrator and judge.
4
Trace the ikhtilaf with Ibn Abi Layla
Ibn Abi Layla held that the arbitrator's judgment cannot be annulled unless it violates shari'a, unlike the Hanafi position giving the judge discretion.
The practical difference (thamarah) is that Hanafi judges have greater flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التحكيم حكمه. ولكل المحكمين أن يرجع قبل حكمه، فإن حكم لزمهما، وأمضى القاضي حكمه إن وافق مذهبه، وإلا أبطله وبطل حكمه لأبويه وولده وزوجته كحكم القاضي، لأنه لا فائدة في نقضه بخلاف حكمه عليهم. ولكل المحكمين بتشديد الكاف وكسرها على صيغة التثنية أي لكل واحد منهما. (عيني) قبل حكمه أي قبل حكم المحكم؛ لأنه مقلد من جهتهما فكان لهما عزله قبل أن يحكم بينهما. (عيني) لزمهما لان حكمه صدر عن ولاية شرعية كالقاضي إذا حكم لزم، ثم بالعزل لا يبطل حكمه، فكذا هذا، وأشار إلى أنه يتعدى حكمه إلى غيرهما فلو حكماه في عيب مبيع فقضى برده، ليس للبائع أن يرده على بائعه الأول إلا برضاه. (فتح) وأمضى القاضي حكمه أي إذا رفع حكم المحكم إلى القاضي نفذه إن كان موافقا لمذهبه؛ لأنه لا فائدة في نقضه، ثم فائدة هذا الإمضاء: أن لا يكون للقاضي الآخر الذي يرى خلافه نقضه إذا رفع إليه. ولو أخبر هذا المحكم بإقرار أحد الخصمين أو بعدالة الشهود وهما على حالهما، يقبل قوله؛ لأن الولاية قائمة وإن أخبر بالحكم بعد العزل لا يقبل؛ لانقضاء الولاية. وإلا أبطله أي وإن لم يوافق مذهبه بأن حكم بقول أبي يوسف مثلا، والقاضي يرى قول أبي حنيفة أبطله إن شاء؛ لأن حكمه لا يلزم القاضي؛ لعدم التحكيم من جهته، بخلاف ما إذا رفع إليه حكم حاكم حيث لا يبطله وإن خالف مذهبه، إلا أن يخالف الأدلة الشرعية كما مر، وقال ابن أبي ليلى هو بمنزلة المولى من جهة الإمام حتى لا يكون لأحد أن ينقض حكمه ما لم يخالف الدليل الشرعي، ومعنى "أبطله" أي لم يعمل به، وقضى برأيه، ولا يشترط أن يقول: أبطلت حكمه. (فتح) وبطل حكمه إلخ لأن أهلية الشهادة شرط للقضاء والشهادة لهؤلاء غير مقبولة، فكذلك الحكم سواء كان من المحكم أو من القاضي إلا إذا ورد عليه كتاب قاض لمن لا تقبل شهادته له فيجوز قضاؤه لهم. (فتح، عناية) كحكم القاضي: أي كما يبطل حكم القاضي لهؤلاء للتهمة (عيني) عليهم أي على أبويه وولده وزوجته حيث يصح لعدم التهمة كالشهادة. (ط، عيني)
Chapter of Arbitration. Its ruling. And each of the two arbitrators can revoke before his judgment. And if he judges, it binds them. And the judge executes his judgment if it agrees with his school; otherwise he annuls it. And his judgment is invalid regarding his parents, his child, and his wife, like the judgment of a judge, because there is no benefit in annulling it, unlike his judgment against them. And 'each of the two arbitrators' (with shadda on the kaf and kasra, in the dual form) means each one of them. (Ayni) 'before his judgment' means before the judgment of the arbitrator; because he is a delegate from them, so they have the right to dismiss him before he judges between them. (Ayni) 'binds them' because his judgment is based on a valid authority, like a judge: if he judges, it is binding, and then dismissal does not nullify his judgment. So too this. And he alluded that his judgment extends to others: so if they arbitrated him regarding a defect in a sold item and he ruled for its return, the seller cannot return it to his original seller except with his consent. (Fath) 'And the judge executes his judgment' means if the arbitrator's judgment is brought to the judge, he executes it if it agrees with his school; because there is no benefit in annulling it. Then the benefit of this execution is that another judge who holds a different view cannot annul it if it is brought to him. And if this arbitrator reports an admission from one of the litigants or the uprightness of witnesses while they are still in that state, his statement is accepted because the authority still exists. But if he reports the judgment after dismissal, it is not accepted because the authority has ended. 'Otherwise he annuls it' meaning if it does not agree with his school, e.g., the arbitrator ruled according to Abu Yusuf's saying while the judge holds Abu Hanifa's saying, he annuls it if he wishes; because his judgment does not bind the judge, since there was no arbitration from his side, unlike when the judgment of a judge is brought to him, where he does not annul it even if it contradicts his school, unless it violates the legal proofs as previously mentioned. And Ibn Abi Layla said: He is like an appointed authority from the side of the imam, such that no one can annul his judgment unless it violates the legal proof. And the meaning of 'he annuls it' is that he does not act upon it and decides according to his own opinion, and it is not required to say 'I have annulled his judgment'. (Fath) 'And his judgment is invalid' etc. because qualification for testimony is a condition for judgment, and testimony for these people is unacceptable. So too judgment, whether from an arbitrator or from a judge, except when a letter from a judge comes to one for whom his testimony is not acceptable, then it is permissible to judge for them. (Fath, Inaya) 'Like the judgment of a judge' meaning just as the judgment of a judge is invalid for these people due to suspicion. (Ayni) 'Against them' i.e., his parents, child, and wife, where it is valid due to lack of suspicion, like testimony. (T, Ayni).
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on arbitration (tahkim) in Kitab al-Qada begins by stating the general ruling: each of the two parties (the litigants) may revoke the arbitrator's authority before he gives judgment. This is because the arbitrator is merely a delegate from them, and they have the right to dismiss their own delegate. However, once the arbitrator has delivered his judgment, it becomes binding on them, and subsequent revocation does not invalidate it. This is analogous to a judge's judgment, which remains binding even after the judge is dismissed. The text then addresses the role of the judge: when the arbitrator's judgment is brought to the judge, he enforces it only if it agrees with his own madhhab (school of thought). If it does not—for instance, if the arbitrator followed Abu Yusuf's view while the judge follows Abu Hanifa's—the judge may annul it. This is because the arbitrator derived his authority from the litigants, not from the judge, so his judgment has no binding force over the judge. In contrast, a judgment from a properly appointed qadi cannot be annulled by another qadi merely due to madhhab disagreement, unless it clearly violates definitive shari'a proofs. The passage also notes a special rule: the arbitrator's judgment is invalid concerning his own parents, children, and wife, due to the likelihood of bias (tuhma). This applies to judges as well, except that a judge may judge for them if a written letter is received from another qadi. Finally, the text mentions the view of Ibn Abi Layla, who held that an arbitrator's judgment is as inviolable as that of an appointed governor, such that no one can annul it unless it contradicts the shari'a. The Hanafi position, however, grants the judge discretion to annul if the judgment deviates from his own school.

Key Terms
التحكيم (al-tahkim): Arbitration: referring the dispute to a private individual (the arbitrator) chosen by both litigants to resolve their conflict.
المحكم (al-muhakkam): The arbitrator: the person chosen by the litigants to judge their case.
الولاية (al-wilaya): Authority or jurisdiction: the arbitrator has a delegated authority from the litigants to decide their case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the arbitrator was not qualified
B) When the judgment contradicts the judge's own madhhab
C) Only when the judgment violates explicit Qur'anic texts
D) Never; the arbitrator's judgment is always binding

Key Takeaway
A judge may annul an arbitrator's judgment if it disagrees with his own madhhab, a rule rooted in the limited source of the arbitrator's authority.

Consistency of Claims: Changing from Gift to Purchase in Court
Ruling on evidence when claimant changes basis of claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting evidence when a claimant changes the basis of his claim and apply it to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
A claimant cannot switch his basis of claim mid-case; evidence for a different claim does not prove the original.

This lesson examines a case from Kitab al-Qada where a person claims a house by gift but, when asked for evidence, presents proof of a later purchase. The ruling is that his evidence is not accepted. This illustrates a key procedural principle: the claim (da'wa) must be consistent, and evidence must match the claim. Understanding this helps maintain fairness in litigation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim
The claimant originally claimed that the house was gifted to him and delivered in Ramadan. This is the basis of his lawsuit.
2
Identify the new claim
When asked for evidence, the claimant instead presented evidence of a purchase in Shaban, claiming he bought the house from the same alleged donor.
3
Analyze the rejection
The judge rejected the evidence because the new claim (purchase) does not prove the original claim (gift). The claimant cannot change the basis of his case; each claim must stand on its own evidence.
The ruling prevents prejudice to the defendant, who prepared a defense against the gift claim.
4
Derive the principle
A claimant must present evidence for the exact claim he made. Switching to a different claim mid-case is not permitted; the evidence for the new claim is inadmissible for the original case.
5
Apply to similar scenarios
Consider a claimant who first claims a debt from a loan, then presents evidence of a sale. The same ruling applies: evidence of sale does not prove the loan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعى دارا في يد رجل أنه وهبها له في وقت كذا، فسئل البينة، صورته ادعى رجل داراً في يد رجل آخر ذي اليد أن الآخر وهب الدار للمدعي، وسلمها إليه في رمضان مثلاً، وقبضها المدعي لنفسه، فطلب القاضي البينة من المدعي على هذه الدعوى، فقال المدعي: لم تكن لي بينة في حق الهبة، ولكن لي بينة على الشراء منه، وذلك لأني طالبت المدعى عليه هذا بأن أتصرف في هذه الدار؛ لأنها ملكي بطريق الهبة والتسليم منه، فحينئذ جحدني المدعى عليه، فاضطررت إلى شراء الدار هذه منه، فاشتريتها من الواهب في شعبان مثلاً، وبرهن على ذلك، لا يقبل برهانه ولا يقضى له بالدار؛ - للقصارين
A man claimed a house in the possession of another man that the latter had gifted it to him at such-and-such a time, and he was asked for evidence. The judge asked the claimant for evidence of this claim. The claimant said: I had no evidence regarding the gift, but I have evidence of purchase from him, because I demanded of this defendant that I might use this house, since it is my property by way of gift and delivery from him, but the defendant denied me, so I was compelled to purchase this house from him, so I bought it from the donor in Shaban, for example, and he presented evidence of that. The ruling: his evidence is not accepted and he is not awarded the house - for the ...)

Commentary
This passage presents a scenario from the book of judiciary (Kitab al-Qada). The matn states that a person claims a house is his by gift, but when asked for evidence, he instead presents evidence of a later purchase. The commentary explains the scenario: the claimant initially claimed the house was gifted and delivered to him, but when the defendant denied it, the claimant bought the house from the same alleged donor and now tries to use that purchase as evidence. The ruling is that this evidence is not accepted, and the house is not given to him. The reason implied is that the claim has changed: he cannot switch from claiming gift to claiming purchase in the same lawsuit, or because the purchase is a different transaction and does not prove the original gift. The text cuts off with 'للقصارين' (for the...), possibly referring to a principle or a specific category like 'القاصرين' (the minors) or a continuation. This is an important ruling about consistency in legal claims.

Key Terms

هِبَة (hibah): Gift; a contract of transfer of ownership without consideration

Key Takeaway
Evidence must match the original claim; a change in claim nullifies the evidence for the new claim in that lawsuit.

Evidence of Purchase After Claimed Hibah
Ruling on the admissibility of purchase evidence relative to the timing of a claimed hibah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule regarding acceptance of evidence of purchase when a gift is claimed, and apply it to identify contradictory claims in judicial disputes.

Core Idea
Timing resolves contradiction: purchase before claimed gift rejected, after accepted.

In judicial settings, claims must be consistent. This passage illustrates how the Hanafi school handles contradictory evidence when a plaintiff first claims a gift but then proves purchase. The ruling hinges on the timing of the purchase relative to the claimed gift date. Understanding this rule helps prevent abuse of the legal process through inconsistent claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the initial claim
The plaintiff claims that a house in the defendant's possession was gifted to him at a specific time (the hibah date). This means the plaintiff asserts ownership by gift from that date onward.
2
Identify the defendant's response and the plaintiff's counter
The defendant denies the gift. The plaintiff then responds that the defendant 'jihadani' (denied me), so he bought the house. He brings evidence of purchase (a sale contract or witnesses).
The phrase 'jihadani' may or may not be stated, but the ruling applies regardless.
3
Classify the evidence and note the timing
Determine the date of the purchase evidence. The ruling distinguishes between evidence of purchase that is before the claimed hibah date and evidence after that date.
4
Apply the rule based on timing
If the purchase date is before the hibah date, the evidence is not accepted because it contradicts the claim that he became owner by gift. If it is after, the evidence is accepted because the two claims can be reconciled: the gift may have failed, leading to a later purchase.
5
Understand the rationale
The key principle is consistency in claims. A party cannot claim ownership by two incompatible means covering the same period. Timing determines whether the claims are compatible or contradictory.
This is a manifestation of the broader Hanafi principle that claims must be consistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعى دارا في يد رجل أنه وهبها له في وقت كذا، فسئل البينة، فقال: جحدنيها فاشتريتها، وبرهن على الشراء قبل الوقت الذي يدعي فيه الهبة لا تقبل، وبعده تقبل.
He claimed a house in the possession of a man that he had gifted it to him at a certain time, so he was asked for evidence. He said: He denied it to me, so I bought it, and he provided evidence of purchase before the time in which he claimed the gift; it is not accepted. And after it, it is accepted.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This is a judicial ruling about contradictory claims in court. The scenario: a plaintiff claims that a house currently in the defendant's possession was gifted to him (the plaintiff) by the defendant and delivered at a specific time. The judge asks for evidence. The plaintiff brings evidence of purchase, stating that because the defendant denied the gift, he (the plaintiff) bought the house from the defendant. But the purchase date he proves is earlier than the time he claimed the gift. The ruling: such evidence is not accepted because it is contradictory. He cannot claim both ownership by gift and by purchase before the gift. However, if he proves the purchase after the time of the claimed gift, the evidence is accepted because it is possible that the gift failed and then he bought it. The contradiction is removed because the two claims can be reconciled. The same rule applies whether he explicitly says 'he denied me' or not.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift; transfer of ownership without compensation, requires offer, acceptance, and delivery (qabd).
البينة (al-bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is evidence of purchase before the hibah date not accepted?
A) Because the witnesses are not reliable.
B) Because it contradicts the claim of receiving the house as a gift.
C) Because the judge does not allow purchase evidence in gift cases.
D) Because the defendant denies the purchase.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qadā', evidence of purchase before a claimed gift is rejected due to contradiction; after, it is accepted as consistent.

Contradictory Claims of Gift and Sale: When is Evidence Accepted?
Rule on accepting evidence with contradictory dates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for accepting evidence when a claimant first claims a gift and then a purchase of the same property, and apply it to scenarios with different dates.

Core Idea
Evidence of purchase is rejected if it predates the claimed gift; accepted if after.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains how a judge handles a case where a person first claims a gift, then claims to have purchased the same property, and presents evidence that seems contradictory. The key is whether the dates allow reconciliation. Let's examine the rule and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradictory claims
The plaintiff claims a gift first, then claims purchase. Note that the purchase is claimed as a response to the defendant denying the gift.
The phrase 'jahanidanī' indicates the gift was denied.
2
Determine the dates of the events
The plaintiff presents evidence (bayyinah) with a specific date for the purchase. Compare this date with the time the gift is claimed to have occurred.
If no date is given for either, reconciliation is possible.
3
Check if reconciliation (tawfīq) is possible
If the purchase date is after the claimed gift, the claims can be reconciled: the person received the gift, then later bought it. If before, it's contradictory.
Contradiction exists when the purchase is before the gift, because ownership would have been acquired by purchase before the gift.
4
Apply the ruling
If purchase date is before gift claim: evidence not accepted. If after: evidence accepted. If no dates or only one: evidence accepted.
The gift claim must involve delivery (qabd) to be heard.
5
Note how contradiction is removed
Contradiction is removed by the opponent's confirmation, retraction of the earlier claim, or the judge's rejection of the first claim.
This applies whether the contradiction is from one person or multiple (e.g., heir and testator).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال: جحدنيها فاشتريتها، وبرهن على الشراء قبل الوقت الذي يدعي فيه الهبة، أي الدار من ذي اليد أي أقام المدعي بينة من المدعى عليه ذي اليد أي المدعي الهبة لا تقبل، وبعده تقبل، ومن قال لآخر: اشتريت منّي هذه الأمة فأنكر، للبائع أن أي الآخر الشراء لأنه يمكن التوفيق يطأها إن ترك الخصومة. أي يجامع الأمة أي البائع جاز لوجود التناقض، وعدم إمكان التوفيق من وجهين أحدهما من حيث أن المدعي ادعى الشراء بعد الهبة حيث قال: جحدنيها فاشتريتها والفاء للتعقيب والشهود شهدوا بالشراء ،قبلها فكانت الشهادة مخالفة للدعوى، والثاني: من حيث الدعوى نفسها؛ لأنه لا تثبت موجب الشهادة، وهو تقدم وقت الشراء على وقت الهبة؛ لأنه حينئذ يكون قائلاً: وهب لي هذه الدار ، وكانت ملكاً لي بالشراء قبل الهبة، فكيف يثبت الملك بالهبة بعد ثبوته بالشراء؟ وقيدنا المسألة بالتسليم والقبض؛ لأن دعوى الهبة المجردة عن التسليم لا تسمع ؛ لعدم صحتها، فلا يترتب عليها سؤال القاضي البينة من المدعي والتناقض كما يمنع الدعوى بنفسه يمنع الدعوى لغيره، ويرتفع التناقض بتصديق الخصم، وبرجوع المتناقض عن الأول بأن يقول: تركته وأدعي بكذا وبتكذيب الحاكم، وكما يكون التناقض من متكلم واحد يكون من متكلمين كوارث ومورث، ووكيل وموكل. (مسكين، فتح، عناية) الهبة بأن أثبت الشراء في شعبان (محشي) لا تقبل لا تقبل به بينته لظهور التناقض؛ لأنه يدعي الشراء بعد الهبة، والشهود يشهدون له به قبلها. (عيني) وبعده تقبل أي لو برهن المدعي في الصورة المذكورة على الشراء بعد الوقت الذي يدعي فيه الهبة تقبل بينته؛ لأنه يمكن التوفيق؛ لأن الشراء وجد بعد الوقت الذي يدعي فيه الهبة، فلا يكون مناقضاً، ولو لم يقل: جحدني الهبة، والمسألتان بحالهما، لا تقبل في الأولى وتقبل في الثانية، وعلى هذا لو حذف المصنف لفظ "جحدنيها" لكان أولى؛ إذ لا فرق في ذلك بين أن يقول: جحدني الهبة أو لا، ولو لم يذكر لهما تاريخاً أو ذكر لأحدهما ينبغي أن تقبل بينته؛ لأنه يمكن التوفيق بأن يجعل الشراء متأخراً. رمز الحقائق: ١٣٢/٢] (فتح)
He said: He denied the gift from me so I bought it, and he provided evidence of purchase before the time he claims the gift, i.e., the house from the possessor, i.e., the plaintiff presented evidence from the defendant, the possessor, i.e., the plaintiff's claim of gift is not accepted, but after that it is accepted. And whoever said to another: You bought this slave-girl from me, and the other denied, for the seller that he may [؟] the other purchase because reconciliation is possible, he may have intercourse if he drops the lawsuit, i.e., the seller may have intercourse with the slave-girl, it is permissible due to contradiction and impossibility of reconciliation from two aspects: one from the fact that the plaintiff claimed the purchase after the gift when he said: He denied it from me so I bought it, and the letter fa indicates sequence, and the witnesses testified to the purchase before it, so the testimony contradicted the claim; the second: from the claim itself because the effect of the testimony is not established, which is the precedence of the time of purchase over the time of gift, because then he would be saying: He gifted this house to me, and it was my property by purchase before the gift, so how can ownership be established by gift after it is established by purchase? And we restricted the issue to delivery and possession because the claim of gift without delivery is not heard due to its invalidity, so the judge's request for evidence from the plaintiff does not follow from it. And contradiction prevents the claim itself and prevents the claim of another, and contradiction is removed by the opponent's confirmation, by the contradictory retracting the first saying: I abandon it and claim such-and-such, and by the judge's rejection. And contradiction occurs from one speaker and from two speakers like heir and testator, agent and principal. (Miskin, Fath, Inayah) The gift by proving the purchase in Sha'ban (Mahshi) is not accepted, his evidence is not accepted due to the appearance of contradiction because he claims purchase after the gift, and the witnesses testify for him to it before it. (Ayni) And after it, it is accepted, i.e., if the plaintiff in the mentioned scenario provides evidence of purchase after the time he claims the gift, his evidence is accepted because reconciliation is possible, because the purchase occurred after the time he claims the gift, so it is not contradictory. And if he did not say: He denied the gift from me, and both cases remain, it is not accepted in the first and accepted in the second. Therefore, if the author had omitted the word "denied it from me" it would be better, as there is no difference between saying he denied the gift or not. And if he did not mention a date for either or mentioned for one, it is appropriate that his evidence be accepted because reconciliation is possible by making the purchase later. Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 132/2 (Fath)

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of contradictory claims in a court case, specifically when a person first claims a gift and then claims purchase of the same property. The author explains that if the plaintiff provides evidence of purchase before the time he claims the gift, the evidence is not accepted due to clear contradiction: the claim of purchase after the gift contradicts the testimony that the purchase happened before the gift. If the purchase date is after the claimed gift, the evidence is accepted because the two claims can be reconciled. The passage also addresses the scenario where the plaintiff does not explicitly mention the denial of the gift; the ruling remains the same. The discussion covers the removal of contradiction by the opponent's confirmation, retraction, or the judge's rejection. It also notes that contradiction applies whether from one speaker or multiple, such as heir and testator. The author stresses that the gift claim must involve delivery to be heard. The key point is that inconsistency in the sequence of events invalidates the evidence unless a later date for purchase allows reconciliation.

Key Terms
التناقض (al-tanaqud): Contradiction in claims or evidence, particularly when a claimant makes two inconsistent statements regarding the same property.
الهبة (al-hibah): A gift; transfer of ownership without compensation.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females in financial cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the purchase evidence is for a date before the claimed gift.
B) When the purchase evidence is for a date after the claimed gift.
C) When the plaintiff does not mention the gift denial.
D) When the gift claim is made without delivery.

Key Takeaway
Evidence of purchase is accepted only if its date is after the claimed gift; otherwise, it is rejected due to tanaqud.

Acknowledging Receipt of Dirhams and Claiming Counterfeit
Explaining when a person is believed if he acknowledges receiving dirhams then claims they were counterfeit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a claim of counterfeit dirhams is accepted based on the wording and timing of the acknowledgment, and classify the types of defective dirhams (zuyuf, nubahrajah, stooqah).

Core Idea
The term 'dirhams' includes good and counterfeit; a connected exception is valid, a separated one is not.

In Hanafi law, the phrase 'I acknowledge receiving ten dirhams' is ambiguous as to quality. If the recipient later claims the dirhams were counterfeit, his credibility depends on how he phrased his acknowledgment and when he made the claim. This lesson dissects the ruling, the definitions of defective dirhams, and the key distinction between connected (mawsul) and separated (mafsul) statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acknowledgment and the subsequent claim
The person first acknowledges receiving 'ten dirhams' (or his due). Then, he claims they were 'zuyuf' (counterfeit) or another defective type. Note whether he specified 'good dirhams' initially.
If he specified 'good', the claim is rejected entirely.
2
Check the timing: connected or separated?
Determine if the claim was made immediately after the acknowledgment (mawsul) or after a pause (mafsul). The ruling differs based on this and the type of defect claimed.
Connected means in the same breath; separated means later conversation.
3
Apply the rule for each type of defective dirham
For zuyuf (treasury-rejected) and nubahrajah (trader-rejected): if connected, he is believed with an oath; if separated, not. For stooqah (heavily adulterated): he is believed only if connected, because the term 'dirhams' does not apply to stooqah.
Stooqah is not considered 'dirhams' due to prevalent adulteration.
4
Understand the linguistic principle
The word 'dirhams' covers all types unless qualified. The 'connected' claim is treated as an exception from the original statement, akin to 'I owe 1000 except 100'.
If disconnected, the exception is invalid because it contradicts the original assertion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أقرّ بقبض عشرة ثم ادعى أنها زيوف، صدق مع يمينه. المقر لأن اسم الدراهم يعمها المقر أي العشرة (commentary continues...) اسم الدراهم يقع على الجياد والزيوف والنبهرجة، ولهذا لو تجوز بهما في الصرف والسلم جاز، والقبض لا يختص بالجياد، فيصدق في إنكاره قبض حقه مع يمينه وقيد بالزيوف؛ لأنه لو قال: إنها ستوقة، لا يصدق مفصولاً، وأما موصولاً، فيصدق في الكل، وإنما لا يصدق في ستوقة مفصولاً؛ لأن اسم الدراهم لا يقع عليها، حتى لا يجوز التجوز بها؛ لغلبة الغش عليها. والزيوف ما يرده بيت المال، والنبهرجة: ما يرده التجار، وقيل: هي التي تضرب في غير دار السلطان وعلى كل قول لم يعتبر في الزيوف والنبهرجة غلبة الغش والستوقة: ما يغلب عليه الغش، وقيل: هي صفر مموه. والحاصل: أنه إن قال: قبضت دراهم جياداً، لا يصدق في دعواه الزيوف مطلقاً، سواء كان موصولاً أو مفصولاً، وفيما إذا أقر أنه قبض الثمن أو حقه أو استوفى، ثم ادعى أنها زيوف ينظر ، فإن كان مفصولاً لا يصدق، وإن كان موصولاً يصدق، ووجه الفرق أن قوله قبضت ما لي عليه أو حقي إقرار بقبض القدر والجودة بلفظ واحد، فإذا استثنى الجودة فقد استثنى البعض من الجملة، فيصح موصولاً، كما لو قال علي ألف إلا مائة، أما إذا قال: قبضت عشرة جياداً بلفظ على حدة، فإذا قال إلا أنها زيوف فقد استثنى الكل من الكل في حق الجودة، وذلك باطل وإن كان موصولاً. (فتح)
And whoever acknowledges receipt of ten (dirhams) then claims they were counterfeit (zuyuf), he is believed with his oath — because the name 'dirhams' includes them. The name 'dirhams' applies to good, counterfeit, and substandard (nubahrajah) dirhams. Therefore, if one uses them metaphorically in exchange and salam, it is permissible. And receipt is not specific to good dirhams, so he is believed in his denial of receiving his right with his oath. And it is restricted to counterfeit because if he said they were 'stooqah' (excessively counterfeit), he is not believed if separated (mafsul), but if connected (mawsul) he is believed in all. And he is not believed in stooqah when separated because the name 'dirhams' does not apply to them, as they are not used metaphorically due to the prevalence of adulteration. And zuyuf are what the treasury rejects, nubahrajah are what traders reject, and some say they are minted outside the sultan's mint, and in all definitions, prevalence of adulteration is not considered for zuyuf and nubahrajah. Stooqah is what has prevalent adulteration, some say it is brass-coated. The conclusion: If he said 'I received good dirhams', he is not believed in his claim of counterfeit absolutely, whether connected or separated. If he acknowledged receipt of the price or his right or satisfaction, then claimed they were counterfeit, it is examined: if separated, he is not believed; if connected, he is believed. The difference is that his saying 'I received what is due to me' is an acknowledgment of receipt of quantity and quality in one phrase. So if he excepts the quality, he excepts part from the whole, valid when connected, like saying 'I owe one thousand except one hundred'. But if he said 'I received ten good dirhams' separately, then says 'except they are counterfeit', he excepts the whole from the whole regarding quality, which is invalid even if connected. (Fath)
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage discusses a case in which a person acknowledges receiving ten dirhams (as a loan, price, etc.) and later claims they were counterfeit (zuyuf). The ruling is that he is believed with an oath because the term 'dirhams' generally includes both good and counterfeit ones. However, if he initially specified 'good dirhams' (jiyad), then he cannot later claim they were counterfeit. The text distinguishes between 'connected' (mawsul) and 'separated' (mafsul) statements. If the claim of counterfeit is made in the same breath (connected), it is like an exception and is valid; if after a pause (separated), it is not accepted. The passage also defines different types of defective dirhams: zuyuf (counterfeit rejected by treasury), nubahrajah (rejected by traders), and stooqah (prevalently adulterated). For stooqah, the ruling differs because the term dirham does not apply to them. The key principle is that the acknowledgment's wording determines whether a subsequent claim of inferior quality is accepted.

Key Terms
الزيوف (al-zuyuf): Counterfeit dirhams rejected by the public treasury.
النبهربة (al-nubahrajah): Substandard dirhams rejected by traders, often minted outside official mints.
الستوقة (al-stooqah): Dirhams with prevalent adulteration, so-called because they are heavily counterfeit.
موصول (mawsul): Connected statement; the claim of counterfeit is made immediately after the acknowledgment without a break.
مفصول (mafsul): Separated statement; the claim of counterfeit is made after a pause or later conversation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person acknowledges receiving 'ten dirhams' (without specifying quality). He then immediately says 'but they were stooqah'. Is he believed?
A) Yes, because the claim is connected and he swears an oath.
B) No, because the term 'dirhams' does not apply to stooqah.
C) Yes, because stooqah is a type of dirham.
D) No, because he should have specified the defect before acknowledging.

Key Takeaway
The credibility of a subsequent claim of counterfeit dirhams depends on whether the claim was connected to the acknowledgment and whether the initial acknowledgment specified quality; the term 'dirhams' covers zuyuf and nubahrajah but not stooqah.

Creditor's Rejection and Subsequent Confirmation of Debt
Ruling on effect of creditor's rejection and later confirmation on debt acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a creditor's rejection and subsequent confirmation of a debt acknowledgment, and explain using qiyas why the later confirmation is treated as a claim rather than a revival of the original acknowledgment.

Core Idea
Rejection of an acknowledgment nullifies it; later confirmation is a new claim requiring proof, not a revival.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in the Hanafi law of acknowledgments (iqrar). When a debtor admits a debt and the creditor initially rejects it, the acknowledgment is nullified. If the creditor later confirms the debt, that confirmation does not reinstate the original acknowledgment. We will explore the reasoning behind this rule, including a comparison with contracts, and understand the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the factual scenario? Debtor says 'I owe you 1000'. Creditor rejects (says 'nothing owed'). Creditor later confirms (says 'you do owe me'). The Hanafi ruling: the debtor owes nothing. Identify the act: the creditor's subsequent confirmation is not a valid acknowledgment.
The original iqrar is nullified by rejection; the later statement is a claim (da'wa).
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The text provides a qiyas (analogy) comparing this situation to a sale contract. In a sale, if one party denies the contract, the other party cannot unilaterally rescind; the contract remains. But here, the creditor's rejection nullifies the iqrar. The proof is the distinction between a unilateral act (iqrar) and a bilateral contract.
The qiyas is jali (clear) and contrasts the two scenarios.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal reasoning)
Why does the distinction matter? An iqrar is a unilateral act; the beneficiary (creditor) can reject it unilaterally, which annuls it. A contract requires mutual consent, so denial by one party does not automatically rescind it. Therefore, the confirmation after rejection is a new claim, not a continuation of the original.
The effective cause (illa) is the unilateral nature of iqrar.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (scholarly difference)
The Hanafi position is that the later confirmation does not reinstate the debt. Other schools may treat it as reviving the original acknowledgment. However, according to the Hanafi school, the creditor must provide evidence (bayyinah) or obtain a new acknowledgment from the debtor.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قال لآخر: لك عليّ ألف درهم، فردّه ثم صدقه، فلا شيء عليه. فردّه أي المقر له بأن قال :ما كان لي عليك شيء أو قال: بل هو لك أو لفلان. (عيني) ثم صدقه: أي المقر له بأن قال: بل كان لي عليك ألف، سواء كان التصديق في مكانه أو بعده . (عيني) فلا شيء عليه: [ أي للمقر له على المقر؛ لأن الإقرار هو الأول وقد ارتد برد المقر له، والثاني دعوى فلابد من الحجة أو تصديق الخصم. (عيني)] أي لو قال رجل لآخر: لك عليّ ألف درهم فقال المقر له : لا شيء لي عليك، ثم صدق المقر له المقر بأن قال: بل لي عليك ألف فلا شيء للمقر له على المقر ما لم يثبت بالبينة، أو بإقرار جديد بعد الرد؛ لأن الإقرار هو الأول، وقد ارتد برد المقر له، وقوله الثاني دعوى، فلا بد له من البينة أو تصديق المقر، بخلاف ما إذا قال: اشتريت منك كذا، وأنكر الآخر، حيث يكون له أن يصدقه؛ لأن أحد المتعاقدين لا ينفرد بالفسخ كما لا ينفرد بالعقد فيبقى العقد على حاله، فيعمل فيه التصديق، أما المقر له فينفرد برد الإقرار فافترقا وقيد بكون التصديق بعد الرد؛
And whoever says to another: I owe you a thousand dirhams, and he (the creditor) rejects it then later confirms him, then nothing is owed. (Commentary:) 'rejects it' meaning the creditor says: nothing is owed to me from you, or says: rather it is for you or for so-and-so. (Ayni) 'then confirms him' meaning the creditor says: rather I had a thousand owed to me from you, whether the confirmation is in the same place or afterwards. (Ayni) 'then nothing is owed' meaning nothing is owed by the debtor to the creditor; because the first acknowledgment is the original and it is rejected by the creditor's rejection, and the second is a claim, so it requires proof or the opponent's confirmation. (Ayni) That is, if a man says to another: I owe you a thousand dirhams, and the creditor says: nothing is owed to me from you, then the creditor confirms the debtor by saying: rather I have a thousand owed to me from you, then nothing is owed by the debtor to the creditor unless he proves it by evidence or a new acknowledgment after the rejection; because the first acknowledgment is the original and it is annulled by the creditor's rejection, and his second statement is a claim, so he must have evidence or the debtor's confirmation. Unlike the case where he says: I bought such-and-such from you, and the other denies, where he has the right to confirm him; because one of the two contracting parties cannot unilaterally rescind just as he cannot unilaterally contract, so the contract remains in effect, and confirmation operates on it. But the creditor can unilaterally reject the acknowledgment, so they differ. And it is conditioned that the confirmation is after the rejection.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a person acknowledges a debt but the creditor rejects it, then later confirms it. The commentator clarifies that the first acknowledgment is nullified by the rejection; the later confirmation is not a new acknowledgment but a claim, so the creditor must provide evidence or obtain a new acknowledgment from the debtor. The commentary distinguishes this from a sale contract, where one party's denial does not nullify the contract because a contract is bilateral. The key point is that an acknowledgment (iqrar) is a unilateral act, so the creditor can nullify it by rejection, unlike a contract which requires mutual consent.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A unilateral admission of a right or liability.
رد (radd): Rejection of the acknowledgment by the beneficiary (creditor).
تصديق (tasdiq): Confirmation by the beneficiary after rejection, treated as a new claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A debtor says to a creditor: 'I owe you 1000 dirhams.' The creditor says: 'You owe me nothing.' Then the creditor later says: 'Actually, you do owe me 1000 dirhams.' Is the debtor liable according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because the creditor confirmed the debt.
B) No, unless the creditor provides evidence or the debtor makes a new acknowledgment.
C) Yes, because the debtor's original acknowledgment is still valid.
D) No, because the debtor can retract his acknowledgment.

Key Takeaway
A creditor's rejection nullifies the debtor's acknowledgment; subsequent confirmation is a new claim requiring proof, not a revival.

Defendant's Evidence of Payment After Denial: The Hanafi Principle of Tawfiq
Ruling on accepting defendant's evidence of payment after denying the debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of tawfiq to determine when a defendant's evidence of payment is accepted after initially denying the debt, and distinguish the Hanafi position from Zufar's objection.

Core Idea
A defendant's evidence of payment after denial is accepted if reconciliation is possible — Hanafi prioritizes substance over apparent contradiction.

Imagine a case: the plaintiff claims a debt, the defendant flatly denies owing anything. Then the defendant produces witnesses that he paid the debt. At first glance, this seems contradictory. The Hanafi school, however, accepts this evidence if the two statements can be reconciled. In this lesson, we unpack the reasoning behind this ruling and the intra-Hanafi dispute with Zufar, learning how the principle of tawfiq (reconciliation) operates in judicial evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
The defendant initially denies any obligation (e.g., 'You never had anything owed to me'), then later produces evidence that he paid or was acquitted of the claimed debt.
The denial is absolute — no acknowledgment of debt.
2
Locate the ruling
The text states: 'He (the defendant) if he proves payment or acquittance, it is accepted.' This is the general Hanafi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
The ruling is restricted to claims of payment after denial, not after acknowledgment.
3
Identify the condition for acceptance
If the payment is claimed after acknowledgment of the debt in the same sitting, it is rejected due to contradiction. If claimed after denial, it is accepted provided reconciliation is possible.
Contradiction (tanaqud) is only fatal if no tawfiq is possible.
4
Understand the dispute with Zufar
Zufar (an early Hanafi scholar) holds that such evidence is never accepted because payment presupposes an obligation, directly contradicting the earlier denial.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; Zufar's view is marjuh (weaker).
5
Analyze the Hanafi response using tawfiq
The majority Hanafis argue that payment may be made for non-debt reasons (e.g., settling a false claim, compromise of apparent debt). Therefore, the statements can be reconciled, and the evidence is accepted.
The commentary cites the phrase 'paid the debt with something false' to illustrate this possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو برهن على القضاء أو الإبراء، قبل، ولو زاد لا أعرفك، لا. وهو برهن إلخ: صورته: ادعى رجل على آخر مالاً، فقال المدعى عليه ما كان لك علي شيء قط، فأقام المدعي بينة على أن له على المدعى عليه ألف درهم، وأقام المدعى عليه بينة على أنه أوفاه ألف درهم، أو على أن المدعي أبرأني . الألف، يقبل برهان المدعى عليه، وقيد بدعوى الإيفاء بعد الإنكار؛ إذ لو ادعاه بعد الإقرار بالدين، فإن كان كلا القولين في مجلس واحد لم يقبل؛ للتناقض، وإن تفرق عن التناقض ثم ادعى الإيفاء وأقام البينة عليه بعد الإقرار يقبل؛ لعدم التناقض، وعند زفر لا يقبل في صورة الكتاب؛ لأن القضاء والإبراء يكون بعد الوجوب، وقد أنكره المدعى عليه، فيكون مناقضاً، ولنا أن التوفيق ممكن؛ لأن غير الحق قد يقضى ويبرأ منه، ألا ترى أنه يقال: قضى الدين بباطل، وقد يصالح على شيء يثبت ظاهراً، ثم يقضى أو يبرأ. (عيني وفتح) ولو زاد إلخ أي لو زاد المدعى عليه على قوله: "ما كان لك علي شيء قط كلمة "ولا أعرفك"، وكذا "ما رأيتك" ثم أقام البينة على القضاء أو الإبراء، لا تقبل بيئة المدعى عليه على القضاء والإبراء؛ لتعذر التوفيق بين قوليه؛ لأنه لا يكون بين الاثنين معاملة من دفع وأخذ أو قضاء واقتضاء بلا معرفة أحدهما صاحبه، وذكر القدوري: أنه يقبل أيضاً؛ لإمكان التوفيق؛ لأن الرجل الذي لا يتولى الأمور بنفسه، والمخدرة التي لم يجر عادتها بالبروز، ودخول مجلس الحكم قد يتأذيان بالشغب على بابهما، فيأمران بعض وكلائه بإرضائه الخصم بالدفع إليه، ولا يعرفه، فأمكن التوفيق بهذا الطريق، وعلى هذا فلو كان المدعى عليه ممن يتولى الأعمال بنفسه لا تقبل بينته. وفي "الكافي": تقبل البينة على الإبراء في هذا الفصل باتفاق الروايات؛ لأن تحقق الإبراء لا يتوقف على المعرفة، ولو قال: لم أدفع، ثم قال: دفعت إليه، لا يقبل قوله؛ للتناقض إلا إذا ادعى إقرار المدعي بذلك فتقبل بينته؛ لأن التناقض من المدعى عليه لا يمنع صحة الإقرار من المدعي.
And he (the defendant) if he proves payment or acquittance, it is accepted; and if he adds "I do not know you," it is not accepted. His proof etc.: its example: A man claims property from another, and the defendant says: You never had anything owed to me at all. Then the plaintiff produces evidence that the defendant owes him a thousand dirhams, and the defendant produces evidence that he paid him the thousand or that the plaintiff acquitted him of the thousand. The defendant's evidence is accepted, and it is restricted to the claim of payment after denial; because if he claimed it after acknowledging the debt, then if both statements are in one sitting, it is not accepted due to contradiction; but if separated from contradiction, then claimed payment and produced evidence after the acknowledgment, it is accepted due to absence of contradiction. According to Zufar, it is not accepted in the case of the book (i.e., the text) because payment and acquittance occur after obligation, and the defendant denied it, so it is contradictory. Our argument is that reconciliation is possible because non-rightful payment may be made and acquittance given. Do you not see that it is said: He paid the debt with something false, and one may compromise on something that appears in court, then pay or acquit. (Ayni and Fath) 'And if he adds etc.': meaning if the defendant adds to his statement 'You never had anything owed to me' the words 'nor do I know you,' and likewise 'I have never seen you,' then produces evidence of payment or acquittance, his evidence is not accepted regarding payment and acquittance because reconciliation between his two statements is impossible; since there cannot be any transaction of giving and taking or payment and receipt between two people without one knowing the other. Al-Quduri mentioned that it is also accepted due to possibility of reconciliation: because a man who does not manage his own affairs, and a secluded woman not used to appearing in court, may be troubled by the fuss at their door, and they order some agents to satisfy the opponent by paying him without knowing him. So reconciliation is possible in this way. Based on this, if the defendant is someone who manages his own affairs, his evidence is not accepted. In al-Kafi: the evidence of acquittance is accepted in this section by consensus of narrations because the reality of acquittance does not depend on knowledge. And if he says: I did not pay, then says: I paid him, his statement is not accepted due to contradiction, unless he claims the plaintiff's acknowledgment of that, then his evidence is accepted because contradiction from the defendant does not prevent the validity of the plaintiff's acknowledgment.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where a defendant initially denies any debt, but then produces evidence that he paid or was acquitted. The Hanafi position is that his evidence is accepted if reconciliation between his statements is possible. The ruling is restricted to cases where payment is claimed after denial; if after acknowledgment, it depends on whether the statements are in the same sitting. A dispute with Zufar is mentioned: Zufar rejects absolutely, saying payment after denial is contradictory. The Hanafi response is that one may pay for reasons other than an established debt. The passage also covers the addition of 'I do not know you,' where the majority (except al-Quduri) reject the evidence due to impossibility of reconciling with the earlier denial of knowledge. Al-Quduri's exception for those who delegate affairs shows the Hanafi concern for practical exceptions. The passage ends with a note from al-Kafi that evidence of acquittance is always accepted because it does not require prior knowledge.

Key Terms
إيفاء (ifa'): Payment or discharge of a debt.
إبراء (ibra'): Acquittance or release from a debt.
تناقض (tanaqud): Contradiction between two statements.
توفيق (tawfiq): Reconciliation of apparently conflicting statements.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts a defendant's evidence of payment after denial if reconciliation is possible, applying the principle of tawfiq to avoid procedural injustice.

Defendant's Evidence of Payment After Denial: Hanafi Istihsan
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidence acceptance after denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on accepting a defendant's evidence of payment or discharge after denying a debt, explain the istihsan reasoning, and distinguish the ruling when the defendant adds 'I do not know you'.

Core Idea
A defendant's evidence of payment after denial is accepted by istihsan—unless adding 'I do not know you' makes reconciliation impossible.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common courtroom scenario: a defendant who denies a debt then later produces evidence that he paid or was discharged. At first glance, these statements seem contradictory, but Hanafi jurists use istihsan (juristic preference) to reconcile them based on real-world possibilities, such as paying a disputed claim to settle it. We'll explore the basic ruling, the dissent of Imam Zufar, and the special case when the defendant adds 'I do not know you,' which creates another layer of complexity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios presented in the passage
First scenario: defendant simply denies the debt, then later brings evidence of payment or discharge. Second scenario: defendant adds 'I do not know you' to his denial, then brings similar evidence.
The addition changes the ruling from accepted to generally not accepted.
2
Understand the dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam Zufar
Zufar argues that evidence of payment cannot be accepted because it contradicts the denial—payment presupposes a debt, which was denied. The Hanafi school counters using istihsan: practical reconciliation is possible; people may pay claims that are not actually due to avoid litigation or as part of a settlement.
Zufar's view is marjuh (weaker) within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the istihsan reasoning for the basic case
The Hanafi argument: 'Non-debt may be paid and discharged. Do you not see that it is said: He paid the debt with falsehood?' This refers to paying a claim that was only apparently valid. Similarly, one may compromise on a claim and then pay or be discharged. Thus the denial and proof of payment can be reconciled in practice.
Istihsan overrides qiyas that would automatically reject contradictory evidence.
4
Examine the ruling when the defendant adds 'I do not know you'
Normally, such evidence is not accepted because two persons cannot have a financial transaction without knowing each other. However, Quduri accepts it if the defendant does not personally handle his affairs (e.g., delegates to an agent). Al-Kafi adds that for discharge specifically, evidence is always accepted because discharge does not require personal knowledge.
The general rule is non-acceptance, with exceptions based on the defendant's circumstances.
5
Apply the ruling to a mixed hypothetical
Consider: Defendant denies debt and says 'I never saw you.' He later proves he paid through an agent. According to Quduri, this is accepted if he does not personally manage his affairs. According to the main view, not accepted. The mufta bihi depends on the defendant's role in his own transactions.
The fatwa position differentiates between a person who handles own affairs and one who delegates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني، فتح) عن وهو برهن إلخ: صورته: ادعى رجل على آخر مالاً، فقال المدعى عليه ما كان لك علي شيء قط، فأقام المدعي بينة على أن له على المدعى عليه ألف درهم، وأقام المدعى عليه بينة على أنه أوفاه ألف درهم، أو على أن المدعي أبرأني . الألف، يقبل برهان المدعى عليه، وقيد بدعوى الإيفاء بعد الإنكار؛ إذ لو ادعاه بعد الإقرار بالدين، فإن كان كلا القولين في مجلس واحد لم يقبل؛ للتناقض، وإن تفرق عن التناقض ثم ادعى الإيفاء وأقام البينة عليه بعد الإقرار يقبل؛ لعدم التناقض، وعند زفر لا يقبل في صورة الكتاب؛ لأن القضاء والإبراء يكون بعد الوجوب، وقد أنكره المدعى عليه، فيكون مناقضاً، ولنا أن التوفيق ممكن؛ لأن غير الحق قد يقضى ويبرأ منه، ألا ترى أنه يقال: قضى الدين بباطل، وقد يصالح على شيء يثبت ظاهراً، ثم يقضى أو يبرأ. (عيني وفتح) ولو زاد إلخ أي لو زاد المدعى عليه على قوله: "ما كان لك علي شيء قط كلمة "ولا أعرفك"، وكذا "ما رأيتك" ثم أقام البينة على القضاء أو الإبراء، لا تقبل بيئة المدعى عليه على القضاء والإبراء؛ لتعذر التوفيق بين قوليه؛ لأنه لا يكون بين الاثنين معاملة من دفع وأخذ أو قضاء واقتضاء بلا معرفة أحدهما صاحبه، وذكر القدوري: أنه يقبل أيضاً؛ لإمكان التوفيق؛ لأن الرجل الذي لا يتولى الأمور بنفسه، والمخدرة التي لم يجر عادتها بالبروز، ودخول مجلس الحكم قد يتأذيان بالشغب على بابهما، فيأمران بعض وكلائه بإرضائه الخصم بالدفع إليه، ولا يعرفه، فأمكن التوفيق بهذا الطريق، وعلى هذا فلو كان المدعى عليه ممن يتولى الأعمال بنفسه لا تقبل بينته. وفي "الكافي": تقبل البينة على الإبراء في هذا الفصل باتفاق الروايات؛ لأن تحقق الإبراء لا يتوقف على المعرفة، ولو قال: لم أدفع، ثم قال: دفعت إليه، لا يقبل قوله؛ للتناقض إلا إذا ادعى إقرار المدعي بذلك فتقبل بينته؛ لأن التناقض من المدعى عليه لا يمنع صحة الإقرار من المدعي. [ رمز الحقائق: ۱۳۳/۲] (فتح)
And if he proves judgment or discharge, it is accepted, and if he adds 'I do not know you', no. And whoever claims against the defendant, i.e., he brings evidence, or that he discharged him, i.e., the defendant's evidence, his slave-girl, he said: I never sold her to you at all, then he proved the purchase, and found a defect in her, another, i.e., the other of the two claimants, so the seller proved, the buyer = i.e., the slave-girl, that he is free from every defect towards him, it is not accepted. The seller regarding the slave-girl, the claimant brought evidence of purchasing the slave-girl from another, i.e., the claimant buyer, because if he had accepted the acknowledgment first, then rejected it, it would not be rejected, and everything that is for both of them when the denier returns to confirmation before the other confirms him in his denial, is permissible like sale and marriage, and everything in which the right belongs to one, like gift, charity, and acknowledgment, his return to confirmation does not benefit him afterwards. ('Ayni, Fath) About 'and if he proves' etc.: Its example: A man claimed money from another, so the defendant said: You never had any claim against me at all, then the claimant brought evidence that the defendant owed him one thousand dirhams, and the defendant brought evidence that he had paid him one thousand dirhams, or that the claimant had discharged him from the thousand, the defendant's evidence is accepted, and it is restricted to the claim of payment after denial; because if he claimed it after acknowledging the debt, if both statements were in one session it is not accepted due to contradiction, and if they were separate sessions without contradiction, then claimed payment and brought evidence after acknowledgment, it is accepted due to lack of contradiction. And according to Zufar, it is not accepted in the form of the book because judgment and discharge occur after obligation, and the defendant had denied it, so it is contradictory. And for us, reconciliation is possible because non-debt may be paid and discharged, do you not see that it is said: He paid the debt with falsehood, and one may make a compromise on something that appears to be due, then pay or discharge. ('Ayni and Fath) 'And if he adds' etc.: i.e., if the defendant adds to his statement 'You never had any claim against me at all' the word 'and I do not know you', and likewise 'I have not seen you', then brings evidence of payment or discharge, the defendant's evidence on payment and discharge is not accepted because reconciliation between his two statements is impossible; since there cannot be a transaction of payment and receipt or judgment and satisfaction between two persons without one knowing the other. Al-Quduri mentioned that it is also accepted because reconciliation is possible; for a man who does not handle his own affairs, and a secluded woman not accustomed to appearing and entering the court, may be annoyed by the clamor at their door, so they order some of their agents to satisfy the opponent by paying him, without knowing him, so reconciliation is possible in this way. On this basis, if the defendant is someone who handles his own affairs, his evidence is not accepted. And in al-Kafi: The evidence for discharge in this chapter is accepted by agreement of all narrations because the realization of discharge does not depend on knowledge. And if he said: I did not pay, then said: I paid him, his statement is not accepted due to contradiction, unless he claims that the claimant acknowledged that, then his evidence is accepted because the contradiction from the defendant does not prevent the validity of the claimant's acknowledgment. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/133] (Fath)
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about accepting a defendant's evidence of payment or discharge after he has initially denied the debt. The matn states that such evidence is accepted unless the defendant adds 'I do not know you,' in which case it is not. The commentary (from 'Ayni and Fath) explains the ruling and the reasoning. The basic scenario: a claimant demands money, defendant denies any debt, then claimant proves the debt, and defendant proves payment or discharge. This is accepted because reconciliation is possible—the defendant may have paid a debt that was not actually due (e.g., through compromise) or may have discharged from a claim that only appeared valid. Zufar disagrees, arguing that payment/discharge presupposes obligation, and the defendant's denial contradicts that. The Hanafi response uses istihsan (juristic preference) based on empirical possibility—such things happen. If the defendant adds 'I do not know you,' normally evidence is not accepted because two persons cannot transact without knowing each other. However, Quduri accepts it, reasoning that a person may delegate payment to an agent without knowing the claimant personally. Al-Kafi adds that for discharge specifically, evidence is always accepted because discharge does not require knowledge of the other party. The passage then notes that if a defendant says 'I did not pay' then 'I paid,' his statement is not accepted unless he claims the claimant acknowledged receipt, in which case evidence of that acknowledgment is accepted.

Key Terms
البرهان (al-burhan): Proof or evidence, synonymous with bayyinah in this context
التناقض (al-tanaqud): Contradiction between two statements of a party that makes reconciliation impossible
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas (analogy) in favor of a more practical or equitable outcome

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a defendant's evidence of payment accepted even after he denied the debt?
A) Because denial is always provisional in court.
B) Because reconciliation is possible; the defendant may have paid a claim that was only apparent.
C) Because the defendant can change his statement without consequence.
D) Because evidence always overrides a party's statement.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh permits a defendant's evidence of payment after denial by using istihsan to reconcile seemingly contradictory statements, reflecting a pragmatic approach to judicial proof.

Contradiction in Evidence: Seller's Discharge After Denying the Sale
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidence contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the seller's evidence of discharge after denying the sale, explain Abu Hanifa's reasoning of contradiction (tanāquḍ), and distinguish Abu Yusuf's counter-argument based on agency.

Core Idea
Evidence of discharge contradicts a prior denial of the contract — the attribute cannot exist without its object.

This lesson examines a nuanced principle in Islamic evidence law (qaḍā): when a seller denies the sale and then later produces evidence that the buyer discharged him from defects, should that evidence be accepted? Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf differ on this question. Their dispute turns on whether a contractual condition can be considered independently of the contract itself. By studying their arguments, you will grasp how Hanafi jurists prioritize logical consistency (tanāquḍ) in the evaluation of evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the litigation scenario
A buyer proves purchase of a slave-girl and finds a pre-existing defect. The seller, who earlier denied the sale, now brings evidence that the buyer had discharged him from all defects. What is the evidentiary conflict?
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and reasoning
Abu Hanifa rejects the evidence because it 'contradicts' his denial of the contract. A discharge modifies the contract's attribute of soundness, which presupposes the contract itself. Since the seller denied the contract, any talk of its attributes is impossible.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
State Abu Yusuf's ruling and reasoning
Abu Yusuf accepts the evidence, arguing that the seller might not have contracted personally; an agent could have sold the slave-girl with a discharge. Thus the seller's denial of his own sale does not negate the possibility of a valid contract with discharge via an agent.
This view is considered but not relied upon.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
In the relied-upon Hanafi position, the evidence is rejected. This means the seller cannot use a discharge clause to avoid liability when he has already denied the transaction. The buyer retains the right to return the defective item.
Practical consequence: the seller's prior denial blocks his use of the discharge defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم تقبل: [بينة البائع على البراءة؛ للتناقض (ط، معدن)] أي إذا ادعى رجل على آخر بيع جاريته منه فقال المدعى عليه: لم أبعها منك قط، فأقام المدعي البينة على الشراء من البائع ثم وجد بها عيبا لا يحدث مثله في مثل تلك المدة كالإصبع الزائدة وأراد ردها على البائع فأثبت البائع بالبينة أن المشتري بريء إليه من كل عيب في الجارية لم تقبل بينة البائع؛ لأن اشتراط البراءة تغيير للعقد من اقتضاء وصف السلامة إلى غيره، فيقتضي وجود العقد؛ إذ الصفة بدون الموصوف لا يتصور، وقد أنكر المدعى عليه العقد، فيكون البرهان على البراءة مناقضاً، وعن أبي يوسف: أنها تقبل؛ لأن التوفيق ممكن بأن لم يبعها هو، وإنما باعها منه . وكيله، وأبرأه من العيب، فيكون صادقاً بذلك [ رمز الحقائق: ١٣٤/٢] (عناية)
It is not accepted: [The seller's evidence of discharge; due to contradiction (T, Ma'din)] i.e., if a man claims the sale of his slave-girl from another, and the defendant says: I never sold her to you at all, and the claimant brings evidence of purchase from the seller, then finds a defect that does not normally occur in such a period, like an extra finger, and wants to return her to the seller, and the seller proves through evidence that the buyer is free from every defect in the slave-girl, the seller's evidence is not accepted because stipulating discharge changes the contract from requiring the attribute of soundness to something else, which implies the existence of the contract; since an attribute cannot exist without its object, and the defendant had denied the contract, so the evidence of discharge is contradictory. And from Abu Yusuf: It is accepted because reconciliation is possible, that he himself did not sell her, but his agent sold her to him and discharged him from the defect, so he would be truthful in that. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/134] ('Inayah)
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This lesson presents a specific case in the law of evidence (qada): a buyer proves he purchased a slave-girl from a seller, then finds a defect that must have existed before the sale. The seller then brings evidence that the buyer had discharged him from all defects. The Hanafi ruling (Imam Abu Hanifa) is that this evidence is not accepted because it contradicts the seller's earlier denial of the sale. The reasoning: stipulating a discharge from defects modifies the contract's essential characteristic (the attribute of soundness), and such a modification presupposes the contract existed. But the seller denied the contract itself, so claiming a discharge is contradictory. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that it is possible the seller did not personally sell her; his agent might have sold her and included a discharge, so the seller's denial of the sale by him personally is true, and the discharge by the agent is also true. However, the relied-upon position is that the evidence is rejected due to contradiction.

Key Terms
البراءة (al-bara'ah): Discharge or acquittal from liability; here, the seller's claim that the buyer absolved him of defects

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Abu Hanifa reject the seller's evidence of the buyer's discharge from defects?
A) Because the buyer's evidence is stronger
B) Because stipulating discharge changes the contract, which implies the contract existed, contradicting the seller's denial
C) Because the defect is not proven
D) Because the seller cannot be a witness

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school does not accept evidence that logically contradicts a prior admission or denial; the attribute cannot exist without its object.
