Effect of 'In sha Allah' in Legal Documents: Scope of Invalidation
Ruling on 'In sha Allah' in legal documents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the effect of 'In sha Allah' at the end of a legal document and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
The phrase 'In sha Allah' invalidates the entire document (Abu Hanifa) or only the last clause (Sahibayn).

A simple phrase like 'In sha Allah' can have profound legal consequences in a written contract or acknowledgment. The Hanafi school internally disagrees whether such an exception voids the entire document or only the immediately preceding clause. This lesson unpacks the arguments of Abu Hanifa and his two chief disciples, and identifies which view is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Understand that the dispute concerns the placement of 'In sha Allah' at the end of a legal document (sakk) and whether it nullifies the whole document or just the last clause.
The document may contain multiple independent clauses, e.g., sale + guarantee.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa holds the entire document is invalid. His proof is qiyas: since clauses are conjoined, an exception applies to all, just as in conjoined phrases like 'His slave is free, his wife is divorced, and he must walk to the House of Allah, if Allah wills.'
The conjunction creates one legal unit in his view.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that only the immediately preceding clause is invalid. Their proof is istihsan: documents are written for continuity and to uphold transactions; applying the exception to the whole would defeat the parties' intent. Each sentence is independent.
They preserve the main transaction (e.g., sale/acknowledgment).
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the sale or acknowledgment is void. Under the Sahibayn, only the guarantee or agency clause is void; the main transaction remains enforceable.
Practical consequence differs in courts.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and tarjih
Later Hanafi fatwa works prefer the Sahibayn's view (istihsan) because it preserves the intended transaction and aligns with the purpose of documentation.
The text states: 'Qawl al-imam huwa al-qiyas, wa qawluhuma huwa al-istihsan.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبطل الصك بـ "إن شاء الله". أي جميع الصك ويبطل وقالت الورثة : أسلمت قبل موته، فالقول لهم، وإن قال المودع: هذا ابن مودعي للصك بكسر الدال - وقد أنكر المدعى عليه العقد، فيكون البرهان على البراءة مناقضاً، وعن أبي يوسف: أنها تقبل؛ لأن التوفيق ممكن بأن لم يبعها هو، وإنما باعها منه . وكيله، وأبرأه من العيب، فيكون صادقاً بذلك رمز الحقائق: ١٣٤/٢] (عناية) ويبطل الصك إلخ هذا نصف بيت من أبيات المنظومة، وتمامه أي المصراع الثاني منه "وأبطلا آخره لا الجملة"، أي يبطل صك الشراء أو الإقرار إذا كتب في آخره إن شاء الله تعالى، حتى يبطل الشراء والإقرار بذلك اتفاقاً، ولا يلزمه شيء؛ لأن الاستثناء مبطل على ما عرف في موضعه، ولو كتب في آخر الصك: ومن قام بهذا الحق، فهو وكيل إن شاء الله، أو كتب في صك الشراء: فما أدرك فلاناً من درك، فعلى فلان خلاصه وتسليمه إن شاء الله، بطل الصك كله عند أبي حنيفة، حتى يبطل الإقرار والشراء. وقالا : ينصرف الاستثناء إلى ما يليه وهو الأخير فيبطل به ضمان الدرك ،والتوكيل ويبقى الدين على حاله؛ إذ الأصل في الكلام الاستقلال في الجمل، والصك يكتب للاستئناف، فلو انصرف إلى الكل، كان مبطلاً له، فيكون ضد ما قصدوه، فينصرف إلى ما يليه ضرورة وله أن الكل لشيء واحد بحكم العطف، فينصرف إلى الكل كما في الكلمات المعطوفة بعضها على بعض مثل قوله : عبده حر، وامرأته طالق وعليه المشي إلى بيت الله إن شاء الله تعالى، أنه ينصرف إلى الجميع، وقول الإمام هو القياس، وقولهما هو الاستحسان. رمز الحقائق: ١٣٤/٢] (فتح)
And the document is invalidated by 'If Allah wills'. i.e., the entire document is invalid, and the heirs said: She converted before his death, so the statement is theirs. And if the depositor said: This is the son of my depositor, for the document with kasra on the dal - and the defendant had denied the contract, so the evidence of discharge is contradictory. And from Abu Yusuf: It is accepted... (interrupted) And the document is invalidated etc.: This is a half-line from the verses of the poem, and its completion is the second hemistich 'and the last part is invalid, not the whole sentence', i.e., the document of sale or acknowledgment is invalidated if at its end 'If Allah wills' is written, such that the sale and acknowledgment are invalidated by that unanimously, and nothing is binding upon him; because the exception invalidates as known in its place. And if he wrote at the end of the document: 'And whoever claims this right, he is an agent, if Allah wills', or in a sale document: 'If any claim of defect befalls so-and-so, then so-and-so is responsible for its removal and delivery, if Allah wills', the entire document is invalidated according to Abu Hanifa, such that the acknowledgment and sale are invalidated. And the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The exception applies only to what immediately follows it, i.e., the last part, so the guarantee of defects and the agency are invalidated, but the debt remains as it was; since the principle in speech is the independence of sentences, and the document is written for continuity, so if the exception applied to the whole, it would invalidate the whole, contradicting the intended purpose, so it necessarily applies only to what follows. And [Abu Hanifa] argued that the whole is one thing due to conjunction, so the exception applies to all, as in conjoined phrases such as his statement: 'His slave is free, and his wife is divorced, and he is obligated to walk to the House of Allah, if Allah wills Almighty' that it applies to all. And the Imam's statement is qiyas, and their statement is istihsan. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 2/134] (Fath)
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This lesson addresses the effect of writing 'In sha Allah' (God willing) at the end of a legal document (sakk). The base text (matn) states that the document is invalidated by this phrase. The commentary explains that if 'In sha Allah' is written at the end of a sale document or an acknowledgment, it invalidates the entire document according to Abu Hanifa, because the exception attaches to all previous clauses due to conjunction. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the exception applies only to the immediately preceding clause, not the entire document. For example, if at the end of a sale deed it says 'and if any claim arises, so-and-so is responsible for it, if Allah wills,' Abu Hanifa says the whole sale is invalidated, while the Sahibayn say only the guarantee clause is invalidated, while the sale remains valid. The commentary notes that Abu Hanifa's position is based on qiyas (analogy to conjoined phrases), while the Sahibayn's position is istihsan (juristic preference), considering the purpose of the document.

Key Terms
الصك (al-sakk): A legal document or deed, such as a sale contract or acknowledgment of debt
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): An exception clause, here the phrase 'In sha Allah', which suspends the validity of the preceding statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, when 'In sha Allah' is written at the end of a sale deed containing a guarantee clause, what is the effect?
A) The entire sale is invalid
B) Only the guarantee clause is invalid, the sale remains valid
C) The deed is suspended until Allah wills confirmation
D) The exception applies to all clauses, invalidating everything

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, writing 'In sha Allah' at the end of a legal document can void it; the mufta bihi view limits the invalidity to the last clause, preserving the core transaction.

Depository Acknowledging Two Heirs: The First Stands
Ruling on depository's conflicting heir acknowledgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a depository acknowledges one heir then another, and explain why the second acknowledgment is invalid.

Core Idea
The first valid acknowledgment removes the depository's hand; the second becomes an invalid acknowledgment against a third party.

When a depository acknowledges a person as the sole heir, that acknowledgment transfers control over the property. If later he acknowledges another as also an heir, the first heir's right already stands, making the second acknowledgment an acknowledgment against a third party, which is invalid. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this ruling and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key acts
The depository first acknowledges X as the son and sole heir of the deceased depositor. Then he acknowledges Y as also a son. The first heir X denies Y.
2
State the ruling
The judge gives the property to the first acknowledged heir (X). The second acknowledgment is not given effect.
3
Trace the legal reasoning
The first acknowledgment is valid and removes the depository's hand (yad) from the property. Therefore, the second acknowledgment becomes an acknowledgment against a third party (X), which is invalid in Hanafi law.
4
Determine the depository's liability
If the depository pays the first heir without a judge's order, he guarantees the second heir's share. The word 'qudiya' (judgment is given) indicates this liability.
5
Extend to other cases
The same principle applies to acknowledgments of debt, deposit, or usurpation: the first stands and the depository guarantees the second.
The text explicitly analogizes to these cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا وارث له غيره، دفع المال إليه وإن قال لآخر : هذا ابنه أيضاً، وكذب الأول، قضي للأول. أي لو قال المودع في الصورة التي قال لرجل: "هذا ابن مودعي" لرجل آخر: "هذا ابنه أيضاً، وكذبه أي المودع الابن الأول، وقال: ليس لوالدي ابن آخر، قضى القاضي بالمال للابن الأول؛ لأن إقراره الأول قد صح، وانقطع يده عن المال، فيكون هذا إقراراً على الغير، فلا يصح، وأشار بقوله: "قضي" إلى أن المودع يضمن نصيب الآخر إذا دفع إلى المقر له الأول بغير قضاء، وقيد بإقراره بالابن؛ لأنه لو أقر المودع بها لرجل، ثم قال لا بل وديعة فلان أو قال: غصبت هذا من فلان لا بل فلان، وكذا العارية، فإنه يقضى به للأول ويضمن للثاني قيمته سواء كان الدفع للمقر له بالقضاء أو بغيره. (عيني، فتح)
If there is no heir other than him, the money is given to him, and if he says to another: 'This is also his son,' and the first one denies it, judgment is given to the first. That is, if the depository in the scenario where he said to a man 'This is the son of my depositor' says to another man 'This is also his son,' and the depository denies the first son, saying 'My father has no other son,' the judge gives the money to the first son, because his first acknowledgment is valid and his hand is removed from the money, so this [second] acknowledgment becomes an acknowledgment against someone else, which is invalid. And his saying 'judgment is given' indicates that the depository guarantees the share of the other if he gives the money to the first acknowledged person without a judgment. And it is restricted [to] acknowledgment of sonship, because if the depository acknowledged a debt to a man, then said 'No, it is a deposit of so-and-so' or said 'I usurped this from so-and-so, no, from so-and-so,' and likewise a loan, then judgment is given to the first, and he guarantees its value to the second, whether the payment to the acknowledged was by judgment or otherwise. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of Judgments (Kitab al-Qada') regarding a specific scenario where a depository (mudi') acknowledges one person as the sole heir of the deceased depositor, then later acknowledges another person as also a son, and the first denies the second. The ruling is that the money is given to the first acknowledged heir. The reasoning is that the first acknowledgment is valid and removes the depository's control over the property; any subsequent acknowledgment is considered an acknowledgment against someone else (the first heir), which is not valid. The judge's order to pay the first heir does not absolve the depository from liability; if he pays without a judge's order, he guarantees the share of the second. This ruling is parallel to cases of debt, deposit, or usury acknowledgment, where the first acknowledgment is upheld and the depository must compensate the second. The source text is from the commentary tradition of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, with references to 'Ayni and Fath.

Key Terms
المودع (al-mudi‘): The person with whom a deposit (wadi‘ah) is left; the depository.
المقر له (al-muqarr lahu): The person in whose favor an acknowledgment (iqrar) is made.
قضي (qudiya): Judgment is given (by the judge).
إقرار (iqrar): A legally binding declaration that creates rights/obligations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a depository first acknowledges X as the sole heir, then acknowledges Y as also a son, and X denies Y, what is the ruling?
A) The money is split equally between X and Y.
B) The money is given to Y because the later acknowledgment is more recent.
C) The money is given to X, and the depository guarantees Y's share if payment was without a judge's order.
D) The money is held by the court until the dispute is resolved by evidence.

Key Takeaway
When a depository makes conflicting acknowledgments of heirs, the first acknowledgment is valid and the depository must compensate the second if he acted without a judge's order.

Surety in Inheritance Distribution: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on surety in inheritance distribution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking surety from creditors and heirs in inheritance distribution, and explain the basis of the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the two companions.

Core Idea
A present right, once established, is not delayed by the mere possibility of absent claimants.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses a key ruling in the chapter of qada: when distributing inheritance among creditors or heirs, should the judge take a surety from the recipient? Abu Hanifa says no, while the two companions say yes. Understanding the reasoning behind each position reveals the Hanafi approach to balancing legal certainty with precaution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its practical context
The text begins by stating that when inheritance is divided among creditors, no surety is taken from them, nor from an heir. Consider the scenario: a person dies owing debts to several creditors, and they present claims. The judge orders payment to the present creditors. Should he require a surety from each in case an absent creditor appears later?
This is a matter of procedure in the Islamic court.
2
Locate the disagreement
Abu Hanifa maintains that no surety is required. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that a surety should be taken because there might be absent heirs or creditors. Identify the exact point of dispute: the effect of mere possibility on an established right.
Note the term 'muwahham' (mere supposition).
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa argues that the present person's right is established definitely (qat'an) or apparently (zāhiran), so it cannot be delayed due to a mere possibility. The Sahibayn rely on the need for caution to protect the rights of absent parties. Also note the sub-difference: if the debt/inheritance is established by acknowledgment (iqrar) or if witnesses state they know of no other heirs, then all agree a surety is taken or not accordingly.
This shows the nuance of usul: certainty vs. probability.
4
Consider the judge's discretion
The text mentions that the judge may delay the judgment until he is confident there are no other claimants. Al-Tahawi estimated the delay at one year. This is a practical compromise. But note that Abu Hanifa's ruling on the surety remains firm.
The judge's caution does not contradict the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ميراث قسم بين الغرماء لا يكفل منهم، ولا من وارث ولو ادعى أي رجل على آخر دفع المال إليه: أي يأمره القاضي بدفع المال إلى الابن وجوباً، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : يؤخذ الكفيل منهم؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يكون له وارث أو غريم غائب، وله: أن حق الحاضر ثابت قطعاً، أو ظاهراً فلا يؤخر لأجل الموهوم، وللقاضي أن يحتاط ويؤخر القضاء ولا يدفع إليه حتى يغلب على ظنه أنه لا وارث له غيره ولا غريم وقدر مدة التأخير مفوض إلى رأي القاضي، وقدره الطحاوي بالحول، والخلاف فيما إذا ثبت الدين والإرث بالشهادة، ولم تقل الشهود لا نعلم له وارثاً غيرهم، وأما إذا ثبت بالإقرار، يؤخذ كفيل بالاتفاق، وإن قالوا: لا نعلم له وارثاً غيرهم لا يؤخذ منهم كفيل بالاتفاق. [رمز الحقائق: ١٣٥/٢] (فتح) وبرهن عليه أي على الإرث فيما إذا ادعى داراً بأن قال: مات أبوه وترك الدار ميراثاً بينه وبين أخيه فلان الغائب ولا وارث له غيرهما، وقيد بالعقار؛ لأن المنقول يوضع عند عدل إلى حضور صاحبه، وقيل: هو كالعقار لا يؤخذ منه. أخذ نصف المدعى فقط: أي لا يؤخذ نصيب الغائب من ذي اليد بل يترك في يده، ولا يستوثق بالكفيل عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : إن كان الذي هو في يده جاحداً، أخذ منه وجعل في يد أمين، وإن لم يجحد، يترك في يده؛ لأن الجاحد خائن، فلا يترك في يده؛ إذ لا يؤمن الجحود ثانياً، وله أن الحاضر ليس بخصم عن الغائب في استيفاء نصيبه، وليس للقاضي أن يتعرض لودائع الناس ولا لغيرها، حتى يأخذها من أيدي من هي . فصار نظيراً لما لو عرف القاضي ملكاً للإنسان ثم رآه في يد غيره، فإنه لا يأخذه منه، ولا يتعرض له ما لم يحضر خصمه، فكذا هذا. وفي قوله: "أخذ نصف المدعى" إشارتان الأولى أنه لا يؤخذ من ذي اليد كفيل؛ لأن القاضي نصب لقطع الخصومات لا لإنشائها، الثانية: أن الحاضر يأخذ نصف مشاع غير مقسوم والخلاف إنما هو في أخذ الكفيل من ذي اليد بحصة الغائب، وعدمه، وقيل: الاختلاف في جواز القضاء بنصفها للغائب، فعندهما: يقضى به له، لا، وقيل: لا خلاف في أنه يقضى للغائب، لكن الخلاف في النزع من يده والترك فيها، وإذا حضر الغائب لا يحتاج إلى إقامة البينة في الصحيح، فيسلم النصف إليه بذلك القضاء؛ لأن أحد الورثة ينتصب خصماً بقيتهم، فيثبت الملك للميت، ثم يكون لهم بطريق الميراث عنه، وكذا يقوم الواحد مقامه فيما عليه، ديناً كان أو عيناً، فيقوم مقام سائر الورثة في ذلك، بخلاف نفس الاستيفاء؛ فإنه عامل فيه لنفسه لا عن الميت، فلا يصلح نائباً لهم؛ لعدم التوكيل منهم، ولعدم قيامهم فيه مقام الميت، بخلاف الإثبات فإنه نائب فيه عن الميت فيما له وعليه، فيكون نائباً لهم أيضاً في ضمنه. (عيني، فتح)
Inheritance divided among creditors: no surety is taken from them, nor from an heir. Even if a man claims against another, order the money to be paid: i.e., the judge orders payment of the money to the son obligatorily. This is according to Abu Hanifa, but the two said: a surety is taken from them, because it is possible that he has an absent heir or creditor. And Abu Hanifa: the right of the present person is established definitively or apparently, so it is not delayed due to a mere possibility. The judge may be cautious and delay the judgment and not give until he is strongly of the opinion that there is no other heir or creditor. The duration of delay is left to the judge's opinion; al-Tahawi estimated it at a year. The dispute is when the debt and inheritance are established by testimony, and the witnesses did not say 'we do not know any heir other than them.' If it is established by acknowledgment, a surety is taken by agreement; if they say 'we do not know any heir other than them,' no surety is taken by agreement. (Fath) And he proved it, i.e., the inheritance, when he claims a house by saying: his father died and left the house as inheritance between him and his absent brother, and there is no other heir besides them. It is restricted to real estate because movable property is placed with a trustworthy person until the owner appears; some say it is like real estate not taken from him. He takes only half of the claimed property: i.e., the absent brother's share is not taken from the possessor but left in his hand, and no surety is taken from him according to Abu Hanifa. The two said: if the one in whose hand it is is a denier, it is taken from him and placed with a trustee; if he does not deny, it is left in his hand, because a denier is treacherous. But Abu Hanifa: the present person is not a representative of the absent one in taking his share, and the judge should not interfere with people's deposits or other things, taking them from those who have them. So it is analogous to if the judge knows that something belongs to a person and then sees it in another's hand, he does not take it from him, nor does he interfere until the opponent appears. In his saying 'he takes half of the claimed property' there are two indications: first, that no surety is taken from the possessor, because the judge is appointed to end disputes, not to create them; second, that the present person takes an undivided half. The dispute is only about taking a surety from the possessor for the absent's share or not. Some say the difference is whether it is permissible to rule half for the absent: the two rule it for him, not. Some say there is no disagreement that it is ruled for the absent, but the disagreement is about taking from the possessor's hand or leaving it. When the absent appears, he does not need to present evidence in the correct view, and the half is handed over to him by that judgment, because one of the heirs stands as a representative for the rest in establishing the property for the deceased, then it becomes theirs by inheritance. Similarly, one heir stands for others regarding what is owed, whether debt or thing, but not for actual taking, because he acts for himself, not for the deceased. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage discusses two related rulings within the chapter of Judgments. The first ruling concerns the distribution of inheritance among creditors or heirs: according to Abu Hanifa, no surety (kafil) is taken from them, because the right of the present person is established definitely. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue for taking a surety due to the possibility of absent creditors/heirs. The judge may delay the judgment if there is doubt. The second ruling addresses a claim by one heir for a house on behalf of himself and an absent brother. According to Abu Hanifa, the claimant takes only his half, and the absent brother's share remains with the possessor, and no surety is taken. The two companions differentiate based on whether the possessor is a denier. The reasoning of Abu Hanifa is that the present heir is not a representative for the absent in taking possession, and the judge should not initiate disputes. The detailed discussion includes the nature of representation in court and the distinction between establishing ownership and actual recovery.

Key Terms
الكفيل (al-kafil): Surety; a person who guarantees payment or return of property.
الغائب (al-gha'ib): The absent person; one who is not present at the time of judgment.
المشاع (al-musha'): Undivided share; a share in the property that is not physically partitioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when distributing inheritance among creditors, is a surety taken?
A) Yes, if there is possibility of absent creditors
B) No, never
C) Yes, always
D) Only if the creditors request it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a present established right is not to be delayed or conditioned by mere possibilities; thus no surety is taken from creditors/heritors.

The Absent Heir's Share: Representation and Possession in Inheritance Claims
Ruling on absent heir's share in inheritance claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when one heir claims inheritance including an absent brother, the present heir takes only his own share and does not represent the absent in taking possession, and distinguish between real estate and movables.

Core Idea
A present heir establishes the estate for all, but takes only his own share; the absent's share remains with the possessor.

This passage examines a specific scenario: a man claims a house on behalf of himself and his absent brother, asserting it is inheritance from their father. Abu Hanifa rules that the claimant takes only his half, and the absent brother's share is left with the current possessor without any surety. The two companions differ when the possessor is a denier. The reasoning involves the important distinction between establishing ownership (which one heir can do for all) and actual recovery (which requires individual representation).

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario and ruling
The text describes a claim: 'My father died leaving this house as inheritance for me and my absent brother, with no other heirs.' The judge orders that the claimant takes only his half. The other half remains with the possessor. No surety is taken from the possessor according to Abu Hanifa.
This applies to real estate (al-‘iqar).
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement
The two companions (Sahibayn) distinguish: if the possessor is a denier, they say the half should be taken from him and placed with a trustee; if he is not a denier, it is left in his hand. Abu Hanifa rejects this distinction and maintains the same ruling regardless.
Key reasoning: for Abu Hanifa, the present heir is not a representative for the absent in taking possession, and the judge should not interfere with property without the owner's presence.
3
Analyze the analogy and exceptions
Abu Hanifa draws an analogy: if a judge knows something belongs to a person but sees it in another's hand, he does not take it until the owner appears. Movable property is treated differently: it is placed with a trustworthy person until the owner appears, but some say it is like real estate.
Immovable property is more stable and likely to remain.
4
Examine the principle of representation in court
The text explains that one heir can represent the others in establishing the right (ithbat), i.e., proving that the deceased owned the property and that the heirs inherit. But for actual recovery (istifa'), the present heir acts for himself, not for the absent, so he cannot take the absent's share.
This contrasts with the obligation side where one heir can represent others in paying debts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ميراث قسم بين الغرماء لا يكفل منهم، ولا من وارث ولو ادعى أي رجل على آخر دفع المال إليه: أي يأمره القاضي بدفع المال إلى الابن وجوباً، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : يؤخذ الكفيل منهم؛ لأنه يحتمل أن يكون له وارث أو غريم غائب، وله: أن حق الحاضر ثابت قطعاً، أو ظاهراً فلا يؤخر لأجل الموهوم، وللقاضي أن يحتاط ويؤخر القضاء ولا يدفع إليه حتى يغلب على ظنه أنه لا وارث له غيره ولا غريم وقدر مدة التأخير مفوض إلى رأي القاضي، وقدره الطحاوي بالحول، والخلاف فيما إذا ثبت الدين والإرث بالشهادة، ولم تقل الشهود لا نعلم له وارثاً غيرهم، وأما إذا ثبت بالإقرار، يؤخذ كفيل بالاتفاق، وإن قالوا: لا نعلم له وارثاً غيرهم لا يؤخذ منهم كفيل بالاتفاق. [رمز الحقائق: ١٣٥/٢] (فتح) وبرهن عليه أي على الإرث فيما إذا ادعى داراً بأن قال: مات أبوه وترك الدار ميراثاً بينه وبين أخيه فلان الغائب ولا وارث له غيرهما، وقيد بالعقار؛ لأن المنقول يوضع عند عدل إلى حضور صاحبه، وقيل: هو كالعقار لا يؤخذ منه. أخذ نصف المدعى فقط: أي لا يؤخذ نصيب الغائب من ذي اليد بل يترك في يده، ولا يستوثق بالكفيل عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : إن كان الذي هو في يده جاحداً، أخذ منه وجعل في يد أمين، وإن لم يجحد، يترك في يده؛ لأن الجاحد خائن، فلا يترك في يده؛ إذ لا يؤمن الجحود ثانياً، وله أن الحاضر ليس بخصم عن الغائب في استيفاء نصيبه، وليس للقاضي أن يتعرض لودائع الناس ولا لغيرها، حتى يأخذها من أيدي من هي . فصار نظيراً لما لو عرف القاضي ملكاً للإنسان ثم رآه في يد غيره، فإنه لا يأخذه منه، ولا يتعرض له ما لم يحضر خصمه، فكذا هذا. وفي قوله: "أخذ نصف المدعى" إشارتان الأولى أنه لا يؤخذ من ذي اليد كفيل؛ لأن القاضي نصب لقطع الخصومات لا لإنشائها، الثانية: أن الحاضر يأخذ نصف مشاع غير مقسوم والخلاف إنما هو في أخذ الكفيل من ذي اليد بحصة الغائب، وعدمه، وقيل: الاختلاف في جواز القضاء بنصفها للغائب، فعندهما: يقضى به له، لا، وقيل: لا خلاف في أنه يقضى للغائب، لكن الخلاف في النزع من يده والترك فيها، وإذا حضر الغائب لا يحتاج إلى إقامة البينة في الصحيح، فيسلم النصف إليه بذلك القضاء؛ لأن أحد الورثة ينتصب خصماً بقيتهم، فيثبت الملك للميت، ثم يكون لهم بطريق الميراث عنه، وكذا يقوم الواحد مقامه فيما عليه، ديناً كان أو عيناً، فيقوم مقام سائر الورثة في ذلك، بخلاف نفس الاستيفاء؛ فإنه عامل فيه لنفسه لا عن الميت، فلا يصلح نائباً لهم؛ لعدم التوكيل منهم، ولعدم قيامهم فيه مقام الميت، بخلاف الإثبات فإنه نائب فيه عن الميت فيما له وعليه، فيكون نائباً لهم أيضاً في ضمنه. (عيني، فتح)
Inheritance divided among creditors: no surety is taken from them, nor from an heir. Even if a man claims against another, order the money to be paid: i.e., the judge orders payment of the money to the son obligatorily. This is according to Abu Hanifa, but the two said: a surety is taken from them, because it is possible that he has an absent heir or creditor. And Abu Hanifa: the right of the present person is established definitively or apparently, so it is not delayed due to a mere possibility. The judge may be cautious and delay the judgment and not give until he is strongly of the opinion that there is no other heir or creditor. The duration of delay is left to the judge's opinion; al-Tahawi estimated it at a year. The dispute is when the debt and inheritance are established by testimony, and the witnesses did not say 'we do not know any heir other than them.' If it is established by acknowledgment, a surety is taken by agreement; if they say 'we do not know any heir other than them,' no surety is taken by agreement. (Fath) And he proved it, i.e., the inheritance, when he claims a house by saying: his father died and left the house as inheritance between him and his absent brother, and there is no other heir besides them. It is restricted to real estate because movable property is placed with a trustworthy person until the owner appears; some say it is like real estate not taken from him. He takes only half of the claimed property: i.e., the absent brother's share is not taken from the possessor but left in his hand, and no surety is taken from him according to Abu Hanifa. The two said: if the one in whose hand it is is a denier, it is taken from him and placed with a trustee; if he does not deny, it is left in his hand, because a denier is treacherous. But Abu Hanifa: the present person is not a representative of the absent one in taking his share, and the judge should not interfere with people's deposits or other things, taking them from those who have them. So it is analogous to if the judge knows that something belongs to a person and then sees it in another's hand, he does not take it from him, nor does he interfere until the opponent appears. In his saying 'he takes half of the claimed property' there are two indications: first, that no surety is taken from the possessor, because the judge is appointed to end disputes, not to create them; second, that the present person takes an undivided half. The dispute is only about taking a surety from the possessor for the absent's share or not. Some say the difference is whether it is permissible to rule half for the absent: the two rule it for him, not. Some say there is no disagreement that it is ruled for the absent, but the disagreement is about taking from the possessor's hand or leaving it. When the absent appears, he does not need to present evidence in the correct view, and the half is handed over to him by that judgment, because one of the heirs stands as a representative for the rest in establishing the property for the deceased, then it becomes theirs by inheritance. Similarly, one heir stands for others regarding what is owed, whether debt or thing, but not for actual taking, because he acts for himself, not for the deceased. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage discusses two related rulings within the chapter of Judgments. The first ruling concerns the distribution of inheritance among creditors or heirs: according to Abu Hanifa, no surety (kafil) is taken from them, because the right of the present person is established definitely. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue for taking a surety due to the possibility of absent creditors/heirs. The judge may delay the judgment if there is doubt. The second ruling addresses a claim by one heir for a house on behalf of himself and an absent brother. According to Abu Hanifa, the claimant takes only his half, and the absent brother's share remains with the possessor, and no surety is taken. The two companions differentiate based on whether the possessor is a denier. The reasoning of Abu Hanifa is that the present heir is not a representative for the absent in taking possession, and the judge should not initiate disputes. The detailed discussion includes the nature of representation in court and the distinction between establishing ownership and actual recovery.

Key Terms
الكفيل (al-kafil): Surety; a person who guarantees payment or return of property.
الغائب (al-gha'ib): The absent person; one who is not present at the time of judgment.
المشاع (al-musha'): Undivided share; a share in the property that is not physically partitioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when a present heir claims a house for himself and an absent brother and proves the inheritance, what happens?
A) The judge takes the whole house and gives half to each when the absent appears.
B) The present heir takes the whole house and holds the absent's share on trust.
C) The present heir takes only his half, and the absent's share is left with the possessor without a surety.
D) The present heir takes his half, and a surety is taken from the possessor for the absent's share.

Key Takeaway
A present heir establishes the inheritance for all but takes only his own share; the absent's share remains with the possessor, and no surety is required.

The Absent Co-Heir: Ruling on Surety for His Share
Intra-Hanafi dispute on surety for absent heir's share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on taking a surety for the absent heir's share and apply the qiyas to distinguish when a kafil is taken.

Core Idea
The judge does not initiate litigation; the absent heir's share stays with the possessor unless the possessor denies—Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn.

When one brother inherits a house with an absent sibling, who holds the absent brother's share? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a surety (kafil) should be taken from the possessor. Abu Hanifa argues for leaving the share with the possessor, while the Sahibayn differentiate based on the possessor's honesty. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of judicial non-interference (tark al-taraf' al-qada'i) and the limits of representation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the contested ruling
The scenario: a present heir claims a house as inheritance for himself and an absent brother, proves the case. The question: does the judge take the absent brother's share from the possessor, and does he require a surety (kafil)?
The passage specifically mentions عقار (real estate) but notes that movable property is treated differently.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that no kafil is taken; the absent's share remains with the possessor. His dalil: the present heir acts for himself, not the absent; the judge does not confiscate property without a claimant. This is established via qiyas: if a judge knows someone owns property but sees it in another's hand, he does not take it without the owner's claim.
The qiyas is explicit: فصار نظيراً لما لو عرف القاضي ملكاً للإنسان ثم رآه في يد غيره، فإنه لا يأخذه منه.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) differentiate: if the possessor denies (jāḥid), the share is taken and deposited with an amin (trustee); if he does not deny, it is left. Their dalil: a denier is untrustworthy (خائن), so leaving the share with him risks future loss.
The Sahibayn's ruling applies only when the possessor is جاحد. Under Abu Hanifa, even a denying possessor retains the share.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and tarjih
The text hints at preference for Abu Hanifa's view with 'عنده' and the statement that the judge is appointed to end disputes, not create them. The present heir cannot represent the absent in taking possession, only in proving the estate. Thus, the judge should not interfere.
Additional note: there is also a related dispute on whether the judge can rule the absent's share for him; the preferred view is that he can, but without taking possession.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the absent's share remains with the possessor indefinitely (until the absent returns or a claim arises). Under Sahibayn, if the possessor denies, the share is removed to a neutral trustee.
The thamarah is clear: Abu Hanifa favors stability of possession; Sahibayn favor security of the absent's property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
داراً إرثاً لنفسه ولأخ غائب، وبرهن عليه، أخذ نصف المدعى فقط. ومن قال: مالي أي أقام البينة [زف ك د؟] أو ما أملك فهو للمساكين صدقة، فهو على مال الزكاة، ولو أوصى بثلث ماله،.. أي قوله هذا يقع أي على جنس مالها على ظنه أنه لا وارث له غيره ولا غريم وقدر مدة التأخير مفوض إلى رأي القاضي، وقدره الطحاوي بالحول، والخلاف فيما إذا ثبت الدين والإرث بالشهادة، ولم تقل الشهود لا نعلم له وارثاً غيرهم، وأما إذا ثبت بالإقرار، يؤخذ كفيل بالاتفاق، وإن قالوا: لا نعلم له وارثاً غيرهم لا يؤخذ منهم كفيل بالاتفاق. [رمز الحقائق: ١٣٥/٢] (فتح) وبرهن عليه أي على الإرث فيما إذا ادعى داراً بأن قال: مات أبوه وترك الدار ميراثاً بينه وبين أخيه فلان الغائب ولا وارث له غيرهما، وقيد بالعقار؛ لأن المنقول يوضع عند عدل إلى حضور صاحبه، وقيل: هو كالعقار لا يؤخذ منه. أخذ نصف المدعى فقط: أي لا يؤخذ نصيب الغائب من ذي اليد بل يترك في يده، ولا يستوثق بالكفيل عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : إن كان الذي هو في يده جاحداً، أخذ منه وجعل في يد أمين، وإن لم يجحد، يترك في يده؛ لأن الجاحد خائن، فلا يترك في يده؛ إذ لا يؤمن الجحود ثانياً، وله أن الحاضر ليس بخصم عن الغائب في استيفاء نصيبه، وليس للقاضي أن يتعرض لودائع الناس ولا لغيرها، حتى يأخذها من أيدي من هي . فصار نظيراً لما لو عرف القاضي ملكاً للإنسان ثم رآه في يد غيره، فإنه لا يأخذه منه، ولا يتعرض له ما لم يحضر خصمه، فكذا هذا. عنده وعنده: وفي قوله: "أخذ نصف المدعى" إشارتان الأولى أنه لا يؤخذ من ذي اليد كفيل؛ لأن القاضي نصب لقطع الخصومات لا لإنشائها، الثانية: أن الحاضر يأخذ نصف مشاع غير مقسوم والخلاف إنما هو في أخذ الكفيل من ذي اليد بحصة الغائب ،وعدمه، وقيل: الاختلاف في جواز القضاء بنصفها للغائب، فعندهما: يقضى به له، لا، وقيل: لا خلاف في أنه يقضى للغائب، لكن الخلاف في النزع من يده والترك فيها، وإذا حضر الغائب لا يحتاج إلى إقامة البينة في الصحيح، فيسلم النصف إليه بذلك القضاء؛ لأن أحد الورثة ينتصب خصماً بقيتهم، فيثبت الملك للميت، ثم يكون لهم بطريق الميراث عنه، وكذا يقوم الواحد مقامه فيما عليه، ديناً كان أو عيناً، فيقوم مقام سائر الورثة في ذلك، بخلاف نفس الاستيفاء؛ فإنه عامل فيه لنفسه لا عن الميت، فلا يصلح نائباً لهم؛ لعدم التوكيل منهم، ولعدم قيامهم فيه مقام الميت، بخلاف الإثبات فإنه نائب فيه عن الميت فيما له وعليه، فيكون نائباً لهم أيضاً في ضمنه. (عيني، فتح المدعى: بفتح العين مشاعا يعني أخذ النصف الذي هو نصيب الحاضر. (ط) عيني)
A house as inheritance for himself and an absent brother, and he provided proof, he takes only half of the claimed property. And whoever said: 'my wealth' or 'what I possess is charity for the poor', it is upon zakat wealth...
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a claim of inheritance where one heir is present and one is absent. The ruling states that the present heir only takes his own half, not the absent brother's share. The commentary explains that this applies to real estate (عقار), not movable property (منقول) which is deposited with a trustee. Abu Hanifa holds that no surety (كفيل) is taken from the possessor, while the two Sahibayn differentiate: if the possessor denies (جاحد), the share is taken and placed with an amin; if not, it is left. The present heir is not a representative for the absent in taking possession. The qiyas is drawn from the judge not interfering with property without a claimant. The preferred position (عنده) appears to be Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
مدعى (mudda‘ā): The property claimed in a lawsuit
كفيل (kafīl): Surety or guarantor taken from the possessor
جاحد (jāḥid): One who denies the claim
أمين (amīn): Trustee; a neutral person appointed to hold property

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on taking a kafil when the inheritance is proven by testimony and the witnesses do not mention any other heirs?
A) A kafil is always taken
B) A kafil is never taken
C) A kafil is taken only if the possessor denies
D) A kafil is taken only if the possessor admits

Key Takeaway
In inheritance claims with an absent co-heir, the Hanafi mufta bihi position (Abu Hanifa) holds that no surety is taken from the possessor; the share remains where it is, and the judge does not initiate litigation on behalf of the absent.

Non-Liability of Judge and Trustee for Lost Price
Ruling on the non-liability of judge and trustee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a judge and his trustee are not liable for a lost sale price, explain the public interest rationale, and identify the exception for deliberate injustice.

Core Idea
A judge is not liable for lost sale proceeds to prevent public harm from deterring judicial service.

This lesson examines a specific Hanafi ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq regarding the liability of a judge or his appointed trustee when the price from a court-ordered sale is lost. The ruling prioritizes public interest over individual compensation, protecting the judiciary from disincentives. We will unpack the scenario, the ruling, its reasoning, and the exception noted in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Read the passage to understand the case: a judge or his trustee sells a slave for creditors, takes the price, then the price is lost, and the slave is reclaimed by a rightful owner. The ruling states that neither the judge nor the trustee is liable for the lost price to the buyer.
The passage is a ruling statement (ḥukm) without detailed proof.
2
Analyze the rationale
The text provides the reasoning: the trustee stands in place of the judge, and the judge stands in place of the caliph. Imposing liability would cause judges to refuse such trusts, disrupting public interests. This is based on public interest (maṣlaḥah).
Note the use of analogy: the judge as deputy of the caliph, and the trustee as deputy of the judge.
3
Identify the exception
The passage ends with an exception: "But if the judge deliberately judges unjustly..." indicating that deliberate injustice would change the ruling. The text cuts off, so we infer that deliberate injustice would trigger liability.
This exception aligns with the principle that intentional wrongdoing breaks the protection.
4
Classify the legal category
This ruling is a ḥukm wadʿī (declaratory ruling) of non-liability. It is not an act of worship but a rule of liability. The ḥukm level is 'ghayr makhruj' (not held liable) based on the Hanafi position.
The hanafi_position_box confirms this classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو باع القاضي أو أمينه عبداً للغرماء، لرجل أي لأجل أداء ديونهم صح تصرفه سواء أخبره بذلك عدل أو غير عدل، صغيراً كان أو كبيراً. (عيني) ولو باع القاضي أو أمينه عبداً للغرماء، وأخذ منه الثمن فضاع هذا الثمن عند القاضي أو الأمين، واستحق العبد، وخرج من يد المشتري، لم يضمن القاضي أو أمينه الثمن للمشتري؛ لأن أمين القاضي قائم مقامه، والقاضي قائم مقام الخليفة، وكل واحد منهم لا يلزمه الضمان؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى تقاعدهم عن قبول هذه الأمانة، فتتعطل مصالح الناس، وأمين القاضي هو من يقول له القاضي: جعلتك أميناً في بيع هذا العبد، ولكن إن تعمد القاضي القضاء بالجور،
And if the judge or his trustee sold a slave for the creditors, to a man for paying their debts, his transaction is valid whether he was informed by a just or unjust person, young or old. (Aini) And if the judge or his trustee sold a slave for the creditors, and took the price, then this price was lost with the judge or trustee, and the slave was claimed (by a rightful owner) and left the hand of the buyer, the judge or his trustee does not guarantee the price to the buyer, because the judge's trustee stands in his place, and the judge stands in the place of the caliph, and each of them is not liable for guarantee, because that would lead to them refraining from accepting this trust, causing people's interests to be disrupted. The judge's trustee is the one to whom the judge says: I have made you a trustee in selling this slave. But if the judge deliberately judges unjustly...
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the non-liability of a judge or his appointed trustee when the price of a sold slave is lost after the sale. The scenario: a man dies owing debts, leaving a slave. The judge (or his trustee) sells the slave to pay the creditors. After taking the price, the price is lost (e.g., stolen or destroyed) while with the judge or trustee. Then the slave is reclaimed (perhaps by a rightful owner) from the buyer. The question: does the judge or trustee have to compensate the buyer for the lost price? The ruling is no, because the trustee acts as the judge's deputy, and the judge as the caliph's deputy. Imposing liability would discourage them from accepting such trusts, which would harm public interest. The exception is if the judge deliberately rules unjustly, which would change the ruling (but the text cuts off).

Key Terms
أمين (amīn): A trustee appointed by the judge specifically for a transaction.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school rule that a judge or his trustee is not liable for the price lost after selling a slave for creditors?
A) Because the judge is above the law
B) Because the loss was not due to their negligence
C) To prevent people from refusing to serve as judges and trustees
D) Because the buyer assumed the risk

Key Takeaway
A judge and his trustee are not liable for lost sale proceeds to protect public interest, unless deliberate injustice occurs.

Conditions for Accepting Reports: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on proof standards for binding reports

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on the conditions for accepting reports in cases involving obligation, and apply the correct ruling to each of the ten cases.

Core Idea
When a report imposes an obligation, Abu Hanifa requires either a just person or two trustworthy persons—higher proof for binding news.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute about the evidentiary standard for reports (ikhbar) that create legal obligations. The matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq lists ten cases where Abu Hanifa requires either a just person ('adl) or two trustworthy persons (musturrayn), while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) accept the report of a single just person. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for applying the correct ruling in matters ranging from sales to marriage and agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Recognize that the dispute revolves around whether a report that imposes a unilateral obligation (ilzam) needs stronger evidence than a mere transaction (mu'amala). Abu Hanifa requires 'adl or two mustur; Sahibayn accept one 'adl in all cases.
The concept of ilzam is key.
2
Analyze the five detailed cases
Examine the five cases where Abu Hanifa's ruling is elaborated: dismissal of an agent, informing the master of a slave's crime, informing the pre-emptor, informing a virgin of her marriage, and informing a Muslim in dar al-harb. For each, note the obligation that arises if the report is acted upon or not.
The obligation is the trigger for the stricter standard.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's objection and Abu Hanifa's response
The Sahibayn argue these are all transactions (mu'amalat) like informing about agency, so one just person should suffice. Abu Hanifa responds that each case involves an obligation (ilzam) from the informee’s side (e.g., the master becomes liable for arsh; the pre-emptor loses his right; the virgin is bound by marriage; the Muslim must observe Islamic duties).
The rebuttal hinges on the element of obligation.
4
Note the remaining five cases and exceptions
The passage lists five more cases where Abu Hanifa also requires the stricter standard: informing of a defect, interdiction, dissolution of partnership, dismissal of a judge, and dismissal of a waqf trustee. It also notes exceptions: if the informer is the opponent or his messenger, justice is not required, and for the Muslim in dar al-harb the most correct view accepts the report of a sinful person.
The exceptions show the principle is not absolute.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, if a single unjust person informs and the informee acts (or remains silent), the action is not binding and rights are not lost; under Sahibayn, it is binding. This affects court rulings and everyday transactions.
This is where the scholar's choice of position matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صح تصرفه، ولا يثبت عزله إلا بعدل أو مستورين كالإخبار للسيد بجناية عبده، الوكيل أي بخبر عدل واحد وللشفيع والبكر والمسلم الذي لم يهاجر. (عيني) ولا يثبت عزله إلخ: أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: هذا والأول سواء؛ لأنهما من المعاملات فصار كالإخبار بالتوكيل، وله: أن في هذه الأشياء إلزاماً من وجه، فيشترط أحد شرطي الشهادة إما العدد وإما العدالة وبيان الإلزام في الوكالة: أن الوكيل تلزمه العهدة على تقدير أن يتصرف وقوله "كالإخبار" للسيد أي كما يشترط العدد أو العدالة في إخبار جناية العبد لسيده عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما، حتى لو أخبره واحد غير عدل فأعتق عبده، لا يلزمه الأرش عنده، وإذا أخبره واحد مستور بالجناية فباع أو أعتق لا يصير مختاراً للفداء، وعندهما: يصير، والدليل ما مر، وبيان الإلزام فيه: أن السيد على تقدير التصرف في العبد بالعتق وغيره يلزمه الأرش. أو مستورين: أي أو خبر اثنين مستوري الحال. (عيني) بجناية عبده : يعني إذا أخبره واحد بالجناية فباع أو أعتق، لا يصير مختارا للفداء إلا إذا أخبره عدل أو مستوران (معدن) وللشفيع أي وكالإخبار للشفيع يشترط فيه أيضاً العدد أو العدالة عنده خلافاً لهما، حتى إذا أخبره واحد غير عدل وسكت لا تبطل شفعته عنده؛ لاشتراط العدد أو العدالة خلافاً لهما، وبيان الإلزام فيه : أنه يلزمه سقوط الشفعة على تقدير سكوته. والبكر: عطف على الشفيع أي والإخبار للأنثى البكر إذا أخبرها واحد غير عدل بنكاحها فسكتت، لا يكون رضاً : عنده؛ لفقد شرط العدد أو العدالة خلافاً لهما، وبيان الإلزام فيه أنه يلزمها النكاح على تقدير السكوت. والمسلم الذي لم يهاجر : [ أي لو أسلم رجل في دار الحرب ولم يهاجر إلينا فأخبره بالشرائع واحد مستور لا يلزمه. (معدن)] فإنه إذا أخبره واحد غير عدل بوجوب شرائع الإسلام، لا يلزمه عنده، خلافاً لهما، والأصح أنه يقبل فيه خبر الفاسق، حتى تجب عليه الأحكام بخبره كما في الرسول لا يشترط العدالة، فهذه خمس مسائل من العشر التي يشترط فيها العدد أو العدالة عنده والخمسة الباقية: الإخبار بعيب لمريد شراء، وحجر مأذون وفسخ شركة وعزل قاض وعزل متولي وقف ولا يشترط فيها لفظ الشهادة، ولو كان المخبر الخصم أو رسوله لا تشترط العدالة حتى لو أضر المشتري الشفيع وجب الطلب إجماعاً، والرسول يعمل بخبره وإن كان فاسقا اتفاقا، ويثبت عزل الوكيل بكتاب الموكل أيضاً. [رمز الحقائق: ١٣٦/٢]
His disposal is valid, and his dismissal is not proven except by a just person or two trustworthy persons, like informing the master of his slave's crime, the agent [is informed] by the report of one just person, and for the pre-emptor, and the virgin, and the Muslim who did not emigrate. (Ayni) And his dismissal is not proven etc.: Abu Hanifa's [view], and according to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]: this and the first are equal; because they are transactions, so it becomes like informing about agency. And for him [Abu Hanifa]: that in these things there is an obligation in a respect, so one of the two conditions of testimony is required: either number or justice. And the explanation of obligation in agency: the agent is liable for guarantee on the assumption that he acts. And his saying "like informing" for the master: meaning just as number or justice is required in informing the master of his slave's crime according to Abu Hanifa contrary to them, so if one unjust person informed him and he freed his slave, the compensation is not binding on him according to him [Abu Hanifa]. And if one trustworthy person informed him of the crime and he sold or freed, he is not deemed to have chosen redemption, but according to them he is deemed. And the evidence is as passed. And the explanation of obligation in it: that the master, on the assumption of acting regarding the slave by freeing etc., is bound to pay compensation. "Or two trustworthy persons": i.e., or the news of two persons whose state is concealed (Ayni) "by his slave's crime": meaning if one person informs him of the crime and he sells or frees, he is not deemed to have chosen redemption except if a just person or two trustworthy persons inform him (Ma'dan). "And for the pre-emptor": i.e., and like informing the pre-emptor, it also requires number or justice according to him contrary to them, so if one unjust person informed him and he remained silent, his pre-emption is not voided according to him, due to the requirement of number or justice contrary to them. And the explanation of obligation in it: that he is bound with the loss of pre-emption on the assumption of his silence. "And the virgin": coordinated to the pre-emptor, i.e., and informing a virgin female: if one unjust person informed her of her marriage and she remained silent, it is not consent according to him, due to the absence of the condition of number or justice contrary to them. And the explanation of obligation in it: that marriage becomes binding on her on the assumption of silence. "And the Muslim who did not emigrate": [i.e., if a man becomes Muslim in dar al-harb and has not emigrated to us, and one trustworthy person informs him of the laws, they are not binding on him (Ma'dan)] So if one unjust person informed him of the obligation of the laws of Islam, they are not binding on him according to him, contrary to them. And the most correct is that the report of a sinful person is accepted regarding him, so that obligations become binding upon him by his report, just as in the messenger, justice is not required. These are five of the ten matters in which number or justice is required according to him. And the remaining five: informing of a defect to one who wants to buy, placing interdiction on a permitted (slave), dissolving partnership, dismissing a judge, and dismissing a trustee of a waqf. And the wording of testimony is not required in them. And if the informer is the opponent or his messenger, justice is not required, so even if the buyer informs the pre-emptor, demand is obligatory by consensus. And the messenger acts on his report even if he is sinful by agreement. And the dismissal of an agent is also proven by the principal's letter. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2/136)
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage presents a significant intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the conditions for accepting reports (ikhbar) that impose obligations. The matn states that the dismissal of an agent is not proven except by a just ('adl) person or two trustworthy (mustur) persons, and then lists five analogous cases: informing the master of a slave's crime, informing the pre-emptor (shafi'), informing a virgin about her marriage, and informing a Muslim who has not emigrated from dar al-harb about Islamic obligations. The commentary of al-'Ayni clarifies that this is the position of Abu Hanifa, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree, considering all these as mere transactions (mu'amalat) like informing about agency, thus allowing the report of a single just person. Abu Hanifa argues that these cases involve an element of obligation (ilzam) — for example, if the master acts on the report by freeing the slave, he becomes liable for compensation (arsh); if the pre-emptor remains silent after being informed, his right is lost; if the virgin remains silent, marriage is binding; and the Muslim in dar al-harb becomes obligated to perform Islamic duties. Therefore, a higher standard of proof is needed: either a just person or two trustworthy persons. The passage enumerates ten such cases; the first five are detailed here, and the remaining five are listed briefly: informing of a defect to a buyer, interdiction of a permit, dissolution of partnership, dismissal of a judge, and dismissal of a waqf trustee. The commentary notes that the wording of testimony is not required. It also mentions that if the informer is the opponent or his messenger, justice is not required, because the opponent's own testimony is against his interest and the messenger is trusted by the sender. The authority weight: This ruling from Abu Hanifa is from the Zahir al-Riwayah, while the Sahibayn's view is from the same sources. The text later mentions that the 'most correct' position for the Muslim in dar al-harb is to accept the report of a sinful person, indicating a possible later tarjih.

Key Terms
عدل ('adl): A person who is just, meaning upright in religious practice and trustworthy in testimony.
مستور (mustur): A person whose external state is apparently upright, lacking known sin, but not necessarily proven 'adl.
إلزام (ilzam): Imposition of an obligation or liability, e.g., the master's liability for compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One just person ('adl) or two trustworthy persons (musturrayn).
B) Only one just person.
C) Four witnesses.
D) Any one person, regardless of justice.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa requires a higher evidentiary standard for reports that impose a unilateral obligation, while the Sahibayn treat them as ordinary transactions requiring only one just informant.

Non-Liability of Judge and His Agent for Lost Price in Sale of Slave for Creditors
Ruling on non-liability of judge and agent for lost price in sale for creditors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the non-liability of a judge and his agent when the price of a sold slave is lost and the slave is reclaimed, and explain the rationale based on public interest.

Core Idea
A judge and his agent are not liable for lost sale proceeds if the sold property is reclaimed, to preserve public trust in the judicial system.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh about the liability of a judge or his appointed agent when they sell a slave belonging to a deceased debtor to pay creditors. If the price is lost after the sale and the slave is subsequently reclaimed by a rightful owner, the judge and agent are not liable to the buyer for the price. The Hanafi school justifies this exemption on the grounds of public interest (maslaha), arguing that imposing liability would discourage judges and their agents from accepting such trusts, thereby disrupting the system of fulfilling debts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and transaction
Understand the scenario: the judge or his appointed agent (amin) sells a slave belonging to the estate of a debtor, to raise money for creditors. The price is received but then lost (e.g., stolen or destroyed). Afterwards, the slave is reclaimed by its rightful owner, and the buyer loses both the slave and the price.
The 'reclamation' could be due to a defect in title, such as the slave being owned by another person.
2
State the ruling on liability
According to the Hanafi school, neither the judge nor his agent is liable to the buyer for the lost price. The buyer cannot recover the price from them.
This is an exemption from the normal rule that a seller warrants title.
3
Analyze the rationale
The reason given is twofold: (1) the agent stands in the place of the judge, and the judge stands in the place of the caliph, so they are trustees (amin) not guarantors; (2) imposing liability would deter them from accepting such trusts, and this would harm the public interest (maslaha) by disrupting the system of debt payment.
This is an example of istihsan (juristic preference) based on public welfare.
4
Consider the exception for intentional wrongdoing
The text adds that even if the judge intentionally rules unjustly and takes the money, he is still not liable to the buyer. However, the buyer may then seek recourse against the creditors (the original recipients of the sale proceeds).
This highlights the strength of the public interest argument: liability is not imposed on the judge even in cases of intentional misconduct, but the buyer is not left without remedy entirely.
5
Understand the authority weight
This ruling is found in later Hanafi works (fatawa mutakhkhirin) and relies on the principle of public interest. It may differ from earlier authorities or other schools that place more emphasis on individual liability.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih (disputed), but within the Hanafi school it is the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو باع القاضي أو أمينه عبداً للغرماء، أي لأجل أداء ديونهم، فضاع هذا الثمن عند القاضي أو الأمين، واستحق العبد، وخرج من يد المشتري، لم يضمن القاضي أو أمينه الثمن للمشتري؛ لأن أمين القاضي قائم مقامه، والقاضي قائم مقام الخليفة، وكل واحد منهم لا يلزمه الضمان؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى تقاعدهم عن قبول هذه الأمانة، فتتعطل مصالح الناس، وأمين القاضي هو من يقول له القاضي: جعلتك أميناً في بيع هذا العبد، ولكن إن تعمد القاضي القضاء بالجور، وأخذ المال، فضاع واستحق العبد لم يضمن ويرجع المشتري على الغرماء.
And if the judge or his agent sells a slave for the creditors, i.e., for the purpose of paying their debts, and this price is lost with the judge or the agent, and the slave is reclaimed (due to defect in title), and goes out of the buyer's hand, the judge or his agent does not guarantee the price to the buyer; because the agent of the judge stands in his place, and the judge stands in the place of the caliph, and none of them is liable for guarantee; because it would lead to their refraining from accepting this trust, and thus the interests of the people would be disrupted. And the agent of the judge is one to whom the judge says: I have appointed you as agent in selling this slave. But if the judge intentionally judges unjustly, and takes the money, and it is lost, and the slave is reclaimed, he does not guarantee, and the buyer takes recourse against the creditors.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage states a ruling regarding the liability of the judge or his appointed agent when they sell a slave belonging to a deceased debtor to pay off creditors. The situation: the judge or agent sells the slave, receives the price, but then the price is lost (e.g., stolen or destroyed) before being given to the creditors. Subsequently, the sold slave is reclaimed (due to a defect in title, e.g., someone proves rightful ownership). The buyer loses both the slave and the price. The ruling: the judge and his agent are not liable to the buyer for the price. The reason given is that the agent stands in the judge's place, the judge in the caliph's place, and holding them liable would discourage them from accepting such trusts, leading to harm to the public interest. This is a public policy (maslaha) exception to normal liability rules. The commentary by al-'Ayni adds that if the judge intentionally rules unjustly and takes the money, he is still not liable (the text says 'lam yadman'), but the buyer may recover from the creditors. However, this is ambiguous; the text may imply that the judge is not liable despite intentional wrongdoing, which might be contested. The authority weight: From the matn and its commentary, this is a ruling from the later Hanafi works (Fatawa Mutakhkhirin) as it discusses public interest.

Key Terms
أمين القاضي (amin al-qadi): A person appointed by the judge as trustee for a specific task, here selling a slave.
الغرماء (al-ghumara'): The creditors of the deceased debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The judge and his agent are exempt from liability for lost sale proceeds even if the sold property is reclaimed, to maintain public trust in the judicial system.

Liability Chain: Executor's Sale for Creditors
Ruling on recourse when executor sells slave, slave is reclaimed, price lost

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability chain when a judge-appointed executor sells a deceased's slave for creditors and the slave is reclaimed or lost, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding recourse from subsequently discovered estate property.

Core Idea
Buyer recovers from executor; executor recovers from creditors. Ikhtilaf on executor's recourse from later-found estate.

This lesson examines a scenario from the Hanafi fiqh of sale and debt, where a judge orders an executor to sell a slave of a deceased debtor to pay creditors. If the slave is reclaimed (e.g., stolen) or dies before delivery and the price is lost, who bears the loss? The text establishes a clear chain of recourse, but later scholars differed on whether the executor can recover from property of the deceased discovered after the sale. Understanding this liability chain is crucial for those dealing with estates and executorship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and sequence
Recognize the key actors: judge, executor (wasi), buyer, creditors, deceased debtor. The event: executor sells slave by judge's order, slave is reclaimed or dies before delivery, price is lost.
The executor may be appointed by the deceased or by the judge.
2
Determine immediate liability
The buyer takes recourse against the executor for the lost price. Reason: The executor was the direct seller on behalf of the deceased, acting as the 'aqid (contractor).
Even if the executor is judge-appointed, he stands in place of the deceased.
3
Trace secondary liability
The executor then takes recourse against the creditors because the sale was for their benefit. Whoever works for another and incurs liability due to that work can recover from the beneficiary.
This is based on the principle that the creditor should bear the loss since the sale was for them.
4
Explore the ikhtilaf on later-found property
If property of the deceased is discovered after the sale, can the executor also claim from that property for what he had to pay? There are two views: one allows it (the 'first' opinion), the other denies it (corrected by al-Sarakhsi). The text prefers the first, but notes Sarakhsi's correction.
The difference hinges on whether the executor's liability arose from his agency for the deceased or for the creditors.
5
Note the overall tarjih
The commentary states 'al-awwal asahh' (the first is more correct) but Sarakhsi corrected to non-recourse. Thus the mufta bihi may vary among later Hanafis.
This shows that even within the Hanafi school, later scholarship can refine earlier tarjih.

(عيني، فتح) قبل القبض : أي قبل قبض المشتري من الوصي. (عيني) على الوصي بثمن العبد ؛ لأنه هو العاقد نيابة عن الميت فيرجع الحقوق عليه كما إذا وكله حال حياته. (عيني
And if the judge ordered the executor to sell it for them, and [the slave] is reclaimed or dies before delivery, and the price is lost, the buyer takes recourse against the executor, and the executor [takes recourse] against the creditors. And if a just, knowledgeable judge says: I have ruled against this person with stoning or cutting or beating, then do it, it is permissible for you to do it. With cutting the hand in theft, in hadd and tazir, by stoning for zina, he [the executor] guarantees and is punished and dismissed, and if he did not intend it, his error is upon the one judged against. That is, if the judge orders the executor to sell the slave for the debt-holders, and the slave is reclaimed or dies before delivery to the buyer, and the price is lost, the buyer takes recourse against the executor because he was the contractor on behalf of the deceased. And the executor takes recourse against the creditors because he acted for them, and whoever works for another and incurs liability due to that, takes recourse against the one for whom the work is done. And if afterwards property of the deceased appears, the creditor takes recourse in it for his debt because it did not reach him. And does he also take recourse in that property? It is said that it is permissible, and it is said that he does not have that, because he only guaranteed as a result of the contract being for him, so he cannot take recourse against others. And the heir, if the sale is for him, is like a creditor, because if there is no debt in the estate, the contractor acts for him. And the first is more correct, but Majd al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi corrected non-recourse, so the correction differed. (Ayni, Fath) 'Before delivery': i.e., before the buyer takes possession from the executor. (Ayni) 'Against the executor for the price of the slave': because he was the contractor on behalf of the deceased, so rights revert to him, as if the deceased had authorized him while alive. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage discusses a scenario where the judge orders an executor (wasi) to sell a slave of the deceased debtor to pay creditors. Two subcases: (1) The slave is reclaimed (stolen) or dies before delivery to the buyer, and the price is lost. The buyer can take recourse against the executor for the price, because the executor was the actual seller acting on behalf of the deceased. (2) The executor can then take recourse against the creditors, because the sale was for their benefit; they should bear the liability. The commentary clarifies that the executor may be the deceased's appointed executor or one appointed by the judge; in both cases, the buyer goes after the executor. Then there is a discussion: if later property of the deceased appears, the creditor can claim his debt from it, but can he also claim the amount the executor had to pay? There are differing opinions: some say yes, because the executor incurred liability for the deceased's affairs; others say no, because the executor's liability was due to the contract being for the creditor, not for the debtor's estate. The text says the first opinion is more correct, but al-Sarakhsi corrected it to non-recourse, showing a difference among later scholars. The commentary also adds the rule regarding the judge's statement authorizing execution of punishment, but it seems to be a separate ruling misplaced here. However, the main lesson is the liability chain in this sale scenario.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): Executor of the will or appointed by judge to handle deceased's assets.
الغرماء (al-ghumara'): Creditors of the estate.
استحقاق (istihqaq): Reclamation of property by rightful owner; here, the slave is reclaimed from the buyer.
عاقد (aqid): Contractor; the person who actually concludes the contract. Here, the executor is the aqid for the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a judge-appointed executor sells a deceased's slave to pay creditors, and the slave is reclaimed after sale and the price is lost, whom does the buyer sue?
A) The judge
B) The creditors
C) The executor
D) The deceased's heir

Key Takeaway
The buyer recovers from the executor; the executor recovers from the creditors; and there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the executor's recourse from later-found estate property.

Judge's Delegation of Hadd Execution
Ruling on judge authorizing another to execute a hadd sentence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a judge delegating execution of a hadd punishment and identify the conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
A just, knowledgeable judge may delegate hadd execution; delegation is permissible.

When a judge issues a hadd sentence, can he authorize someone else to carry it out? This lesson examines a clear ruling from the Hanafi tradition: delegation is permitted, provided the judge meets specific qualifications. We will explore the conditions and consequences of such delegation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a judge ordering another person to execute a hadd punishment (stoning, cutting, or beating). The ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) for that person to carry it out.
The text uses 'faf'alahu wasa'uka f'aluhu' indicating permission.
2
Note the condition of the judge
The judge must be 'adl (just) and 'alim (knowledgeable). This is a prerequisite for valid delegation.
If the judge lacks these qualities, delegation is not valid.
3
Distinguish between intentional error and mistake
If the executor intentionally exceeds his authority (e.g., cuts the wrong hand), he is liable (yadman), punished (yu'azzar), and dismissed. If he makes an unintentional error, the liability falls on the condemned person (al-maqdi lahu) – the sentence remains valid.
This protects the executor when acting in good faith.
4
Apply the ruling to different punishments
The text mentions specific hadd punishments: stoning (rajm) for zina, cutting (qat') for theft, and beating (darb) for both hadd and ta'zir. The same permission applies to each.
Ta'zir can also be delegated, but the consequences of error may differ.

And if a just, knowledgeable judge says: I have ruled against this person with stoning or cutting or beating, then do it, it is permissible for you to do it. With cutting the hand in theft, in hadd and tazir, by stoning for zina, he [the executor] guarantees and is punished and dismissed, and if he did not intend it, his error is upon the one judged against.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling about a judge delegating the execution of a hadd sentence. The scenario: a judge who is just and knowledgeable announces that he has ruled that a certain individual is to be stoned (for zina), or his hand cut (for theft), or beaten (for a hadd or tazir offense), and then tells a competent person (e.g., Zayd) to carry out the punishment. The ruling: it is permissible for that person to do so, and he commits no sin in carrying out the sentence. The reason given in the commentary (from al-'Ayni and others) is that obedience to those in authority (ulu al-amr) is obligatory. However, the text also warns: if the executor exceeds his authority or acts unjustly (e.g., cuts the wrong hand or uses excessive force), he is liable (yadman), punished (yu'azzar), and removed from his post ('uzl). If it was a mistake (ghayr muta'ammid), then the liability falls on the person being punished (al-maqdi lahu), i.e., the judgment itself was correct, and the error in execution is not the executor's fault. The authority weight: This is from the matn and its commentary, likely from Zahir al-Riwayah but with later refinements.

Key Terms
حد (hadd): A fixed punishment prescribed by Islamic law, e.g., stoning, cutting of hand.
تعزير (ta'zir): A discretionary punishment for offenses not covered by hadd or qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The caliph only.
D) Any adult Muslim.

Key Takeaway
A hadd sentence may be delegated for execution only by a qualified judge, with clear liability rules for errors.

Acceptance of a Dismissed Judge's Statement in Financial Rulings
Ruling on accepting a dismissed judge's statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a dismissed judge's statement about a previous financial ruling is accepted in the Hanafi school and distinguish between the four categories of judges.

Core Idea
A dismissed judge's statement is accepted if the affected party admits the judge acted while still in office.

This lesson examines a scenario where a dismissed judge claims he took money from a litigant and paid it under a ruling. The question is whether his statement alone suffices or requires evidence. The passage establishes a general rule that the judge's statement is accepted without oath if the litigant admits the action occurred during the judge's tenure. It then categorizes judges into four types based on knowledge and justice, each with different evidentiary weight, and highlights an intra-Hanafi dispute due to widespread corruption among judges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the parties' claims
The scenario: a dismissed judge tells a man he took 1000 dirhams from him and gave it to Zaid as a ruling. The man calls it unjust. Determine whose statement is preferred.
The affected party admits the judge performed the act while holding office.
2
Recognize the condition for accepting the judge's statement
The judge's statement is accepted without oath if the affected party admits the act occurred while the judge was still in office. This is the key condition.
Admission of the timing is crucial; if the party denies the judge was in office, the judge's statement is not automatically accepted.
3
Understand the four categories of judges and their effect on acceptance
Judges are classified as: (1) knowledgeable and just – statement accepted unconditionally; (2) just but ignorant – must explain well; (3) ignorant and immoral / knowledgeable and immoral – statement not accepted unless witnesses testify.
The judge's personal character impacts the weight of his statement.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad later held that the judge's statement is not accepted without evidence due to corruption. Many later scholars adopted this view, but the apparent riwayah (Zahir al-Riwayah) maintains acceptance based on the judge's authority to initiate rulings.
The dispute reflects a tension between ideal trust and practical corruption.
